ii 
 
ABSTRACT 
 
Baggage processes and infrastructure are being challenged by the increasing passengers’ air 
traffic, and a correlation is made between the growth in passenger numbers and the level of 
bag mishandling. In 2015, more than 26.6 million passengers travelled through Copenhagen 
Airport. Therefore, efficient and reliable baggage handling systems and processes are crucial 
for Copenhagen Airport in order to ensure customers’ satisfaction. 
This project focuses on one aspect of the baggage handling process at Copenhagen airport, 
the checked-in bags. In the main baggage factory BF3, the sorting of baggage is carried out 
by three external baggage handling companies through the use of inclined channels, called 
chutes. The aim of this project is to assess to what extent the organization of the chutes in BF3 
can be optimized, and the problem at hand is denoted by the Chute Allocation Problem (CAP). 
Two mathematical models able to solve the CAP are introduced. With the first model, the flights 
are allocated to the chutes, and all the constraints of the CAP are respected. The second 
model is more performant than the first one, and is actually able to optimize the usage of the 
chutes in BF3. 
The two models were tested on real-life data provided by Copenhagen Airport. The different 
computations showed the need for two different approaches in order to answer to the research 
question of this project. In the first approach, modifications are made by hand to the original 
layout of BF3, and in the second approach, the optimal repartition of the chutes between the 
baggage handling companies in BF3 is determined from scratch. All in all, four more optimal 
layouts are created for BF3.

1. INTRODUCTION 
 
In 2015, more than 26.6 million passengers travelled through Copenhagen (CPH) Airport, a 
year-on-year increase of 3.8% (CPH Airport [2], 2016). This number makes CPH Airport the 
busiest airport of Northern Europe (CPH Airport [3], 2016). To maintain this amount of 
passengers, part of its strategy focuses on the use of efficient and reliable airport operations 
(CPH Airport [4], n.d.). More specifically, operating an airport involves a broad range of tasks, 
each concerning different airport partners and stakeholders, see Figure 1 (CPH Airport [1], 
2013, p.7). Being able to operate each of these tasks efficiently with an optimal use of the 
available resources is thus a way for CPH Airport to strengthen its position as the key transport 
hub of Scandinavia. 
 
Figure 1. CPH Airport operations (CPH Airport [1], 2013, p.7). 
 
The focus of this project is on the ‘Check-in and baggage’ operations, see Figure 1, and more 
specifically on the baggage handling process. In an airport, baggage handling consists of three 
main tasks: moving the passengers’ bags from the check-in counters to the departure gates, 
moving the passengers’ bags from one gate to another during passengers’ transfers, and 
moving the passengers’ bags from the arrival gates to the baggage reclaim area (Nice, n.d.). 
For this project, only the in-going baggage are considered, and they correspond to the 
checked-in bags. 
Moreover, in 2014, the rate of bag mishandling was of 7.3 mishandled bags per thousand 
passengers (SITA, 2015, p. 2). Baggage processes and infrastructure are being challenged by 
the increasing passengers’ air traffic, and a correlation is made between the growth in 
passenger numbers and the level of bag mishandling (SITA, 2015, p.4). Efficient and reliable 
baggage handling systems and processes are therefore crucial in terms of customers’ 
satisfaction. Poor baggage handling can have drastic consequences such as flight delays. 
==PAGE:12==
2 
 
Additionally, every airport has its own requirements in terms of baggage handling, depending 
on the available facilities and the sourcing of the baggage handling process. At CPH Airport, 
two baggage factories (BF) are in use, BF2 and BF3. The critical baggage factory is BF3 and 
is the main focus of this project. In BF3, the sorting of baggage is carried out by three external 
baggage handling companies (Aviator, Menzies and SAS Ground Handling). 
The project concerns the need of using the BF3 facilities in the most optimal way with respect 
both to the baggage handling companies’ requirements and to the layout constraints of the 
factory. To be more accurate, when a checked-in bag, or a transferred bag, enters BF3, a 
sorting process occurs and the bag has to go from its initial conveyor belt to the proper airplane. 
To do so, CPH Airport is using inclined channels, called chutes, connected to a main conveyor 
belt, to sort the passengers’ baggage. Different chutes are allocated to the airplanes, and they 
are used to convey baggage to a lower level where they can then be transported to the proper 
airplanes. The aim of the thesis is to optimize the usage of the chutes at CPH Airport in BF3 
by solving the Chute Allocation Problem (CAP). Without going into details, each handler is 
allocated its own set of chutes, and some of the chutes can be shared by the baggage 
handlers. Depending on the size of the airplanes and the number of expected bags, up to three 
chutes can be allocated to each flight, and these chutes have to be located next to each other. 
The CAP is therefore an Adjacent Resource Scheduling (ARS) Problem with additional layout 
constraints as it is detailed in the following chapters. 
The content of the report is as follows. Chapter 2 comprises a complete description of the 
layout of BF3, and details about the three baggage handling companies are given. The 
research question is presented, and all the constraints shaping the CAP are then introduced. 
In Chapter 3, a review of literature is performed following two main directions. Firstly, problems 
directly linked to airport baggage handling optimization are investigated, and secondly, 
literature regarding the category of problems that the CAP is connected to, ARS problems, are 
analysed. Chapter 4 presents the methodology followed in order to solve the CAP. In Chapter 
5, an analysis of the two datasets provided by CPH Airport is performed. Chapter 6 presents 
two mathematical models implemented, and the process followed, in order to solve the CAP. 
In Chapter 7, the results obtained with the two mathematical models are discussed and 
analysed, and four more adapted layouts for BF3 are created. Finally, in Chapter 8, a 
comprehensive discussion of the findings and the limitations of the project is made, and some 
suggestions of future work are given. 
 
 
==PAGE:13==
3 
 
2. PROBLEM DESCRIPTION 
 
This project has been carried out in collaboration with CPH Airport which has provided all the 
necessary information (two datasets, see Chapter 5). Initially, there were three different 
baggage factories (BF) at CPH Airport: BF1, BF2, and BF3. More specifically, BF1 was only 
used for domestic flights, and is now closed. All the domestic flights are currently handled in 
BF3. BF2 consists of 17 chutes, and BF3 of 121 chutes. BF2 is much smaller than BF3, and it 
is therefore considered that if the CAP is solved for BF3, the same algorithm will easily be 
applied to BF2. Therefore, the focus of the project is on BF3. In this chapter, a complete 
description of BF3 is made, and the research question of the project is stated. 
2.1 
DESCRIPTION OF BF3 
2.1.1 OVERVIEW OF THE FACILITY 
When baggage is checked-in at the check-in counters in CPH Airport, it enters the baggage 
handling system. In this case, the baggage enters the baggage factory BF3. At the entrance 
of BF3, the bags are scanned and they then enter a main conveyor belt on top of the whole 
facility. This main conveyor belt is carrying a set of wood panels, and at most one bag can be 
placed on top of each panel. A set of inclined channels, denoted as chutes, are connected to 
the main conveyor belt. The chutes are used to convey baggage from the belt to a lower level 
where the bags are then transported to the different aircrafts. When a bag arrives to a chute 
allocated to its flight, the wood panel tilts and the bag is pushed into the chute. From the chutes 
the bags are then transported to the airplanes. 
The following Figure 2 displays the main conveyor belt with the wood panels, and two different 
chutes of BF3. 
Figure 2. Left: Main conveyor belt (in red, at the top of the picture) with two connected chutes (in blue), 
Centre: A chute being unused, Right: A chute being used (CPH Airport [5], 2016). 
==PAGE:14==
4 
 
To get an overview of the facility, Figure 3 shows the representation of all the chutes that are 
connected to the main conveyor belt in BF3. An analysis of the repartition of the chutes is 
performed below. 
Figure 3. Repartition of the chutes in BF3 (Yellow: Aviator, 18 chutes. Blue: Menzies, 10 chutes. Green: SGH, 73 
chutes. Red: flexible chutes, 8 chutes. Grey: last minute chutes, 10 chutes. CPH Airport [5], 2016). 
First of all, every numbered rectangle in Figure 3 represents one chute, and 109 chutes are 
represented in total. It can be seen that chutes are organized in rows, where the chutes are 
adjacent to one another, separated in aisles for ease of circulation. Before presenting the 
repartition of the chutes in BF3, it is important to underline that at most one flight can be 
assigned to one chute at a time. Additionally, the amount of baggage checked-in in an airport 
is a stochastic process. Based on the size of the airplanes and the number or expected bags, 
CPH Airport is actually able to estimate the chutes demand of each flight in BF3. Typically, at 
CPH Airport, the chutes demand is as follows: 
- 
Wide-body flights: 3 chutes. 
- 
Narrow-body flights (route with transfer): 2 chutes. 
==PAGE:15==
5 
 
- 
Narrow-body flights (route without transfer): 1 chute. 
- 
Domestic flights: 0.5 chute. This means that, when possible, domestic flights are paired by 
the baggage handlers, and each pair is then allocated to the same chute. When there is 
no possibility of being paired, the domestic flights are left with a demand of 1 chute. The 
process of pairing domestic flights is detailed in Chapter 7. 
For this project, in agreement with CPH Airport, it was decided to only take into account the 
above chutes demands. 
Additionally, wide-body flights open their chutes 3.5 hours before the Scheduled Time of 
Departure (STD) while both narrow-body and domestic flights open their chute(s) 2.5 hours 
before the STD. All types of flights close their chute(s) 20 minutes before the STD. Lastly, 
when several chutes are assigned to service one flight, these chutes have to be adjacent. This 
will be more thoroughly commented on in the following. 
Moreover, when a bag arrives in BF3 before the opening time of the chute(s) assigned to the 
bag’s destination, the bag is placed into a specific conveyor belt used for Early Baggage 
Storage (EBS). Once the associated chute(s) open, the bag is taken out of the EBS and placed 
into the main conveyor belt that sends the bag to its corresponding chute(s). In agreement with 
CPH Airport, it was decided that EBS is out of the scope of this project. 
Finally, from Figure 3, it can be seen that BF3 is composed of 109 chutes divided between 
three baggage handling companies (SAS Ground Handling, Aviator, and Menzies) and flexible 
chutes (in red). Note that the grey rectangles in Figure 3 are last minute chutes, which are 
commented on in the following section. Another repartition is also made between high, 
medium, and low areas (red, blue and green rectangles). These two repartitions are explained 
and analysed in the following. 
 
2.1.2 1ST REPARTITION: ACCORDING TO THE TYPE OF CHUTES 
Baggage Handling Companies’ chutes 
As mentioned in Chapter 1, three baggage handling companies are in charge of the bags in 
BF3: SAS Ground Handling (SGH), Aviator, and Menzies. To be more specific, each of the 
airlines departing from CPH Airport, whose baggage are assigned to BF3, has a contract with 
one, and only one, of the above baggage handling companies. In other words, each airline that 
uses BF3 has all of its passengers’ baggage handled by one, and only one, of the baggage 
handling companies. The baggage handling companies are then allocated a set of the chutes 
in BF3 depending on the number of airlines they each service. More specifically, CPH Airport 
investigates every year how many airlines each baggage handler services, and then assigns 
all the available chutes in BF3 to the baggage handlers accordingly. Looking at Figure 3, it can 
==PAGE:16==
6 
 
be seen that, for the year 2016, SGH is given 73 chutes, represented in light green, Aviator is 
given 18 chutes, represented in yellow, and Menzies is given 10 chutes, represented in blue. 
Flexible chutes 
The eight chutes represented in red in Figure 3 are flexible chutes. These chutes are not 
assigned to any of the baggage handling companies, but can be used by any of them if needed. 
More specifically, if one of the baggage handlers is already using all of its chutes at the same 
time and needs another one, it can use one of the flexible chutes. To be realistic, baggage 
handlers are allowed to use one (or more) of the flexible chutes only if all of their own chutes 
are already in use. Additionally, in this case, baggage handlers have to use the closest 
available flexible chute(s) to their own chutes. For instance, if Menzies is using all of its chutes 
and needs an extra one, flexible chute 66 will be considered first since it is the closest one to 
its own chutes, see Figure 3. 
It has to be said that the use of flexible chutes mainly occurs during days where the air traffic 
at CPH Airport is dense. In these days, baggage handlers experience high peaks of the 
demand, in terms of the number of chutes they need, at different times of the day. However, 
the use of flexible chutes requires more set-up times since the baggage handlers are using 
different equipment (e.g. scanners and electronic systems). Therefore, the aim for the baggage 
handlers is to minimize their usage of the flexible chutes. 
Last minute chutes 
The 10 grey chutes in Figure 3 are last minute chutes. When a bag arrives to BF3 less than 
20 minutes before the STD of its flight, the chute(s) that was / were assigned to the flight are 
closed. The bag then goes to specific chutes, called last minute chutes. If possible, when a 
bag arrives to a last minute chute, it is immediately transported to its associated flight. 
Currently, CPH Airport is using 10 last minute chutes allocated all around BF3. These chutes 
can serve more than one flight at a time and are not assigned to a specific baggage handler. 
Last minute chutes are fixed and cannot be allocated to any specific flights. These chutes are 
out of the scope of the project. 
 
To conclude on this section, the project only comprises the baggage handlers’ chutes and the 
flexible chutes, which correspond to a total of 109 chutes, see Figure 3. When flights are 
allocated to the chutes in BF3, the type of the chutes has to be taken into account. For instance, 
an airline whose baggage handling process is serviced by Aviator can only be allocated to 
chutes that belong to Aviator, in yellow, or to the flexible chutes, in red, see Figure 3. 
 
==PAGE:17==
7 
 
2.1.3 2ND REPARTITION: ACCORDING TO THE TYPE OF AREAS 
Once baggage arrives to the chutes, they leave BF3 and are transported to the airplanes. To 
do so, two different loading facilities are used to load the baggage: belly wagons and 
containers. When containers are used, they are filled with baggage and are then directly loaded 
to the airplanes. However, when belly wagons are used, they are filled with baggage but are 
only used to carry the baggage from the chutes to the airplanes. The two types of loading 
facilities can be seen in the following Figure 4. 
 
Each of these loading facilities requires a specific working environment. Indeed, containers are 
higher than belly wagons so a higher working environment is favoured to load containers. 
Therefore, as seen in Figure 3, three different areas are defined at CPH Airport: high (in red), 
medium (in blue), and low (in green). The red area has the highest working environment, and 
is used for flights that require containers. The green area has the lowest working environment, 
and is used for flights that require belly wagons to transport the baggage. Finally, the blue area 
has a working environment height between the ones of the high and low areas. This area is 
thus suitable for both containers and belly wagons. 
To conclude on this section, when flights are allocated to the chutes in BF3, the type of loading 
facilities needed by the flights has to be taken into account. For instance, flights that require 
containers cannot be assigned to chutes in the low (green) area. All in all, based on what was 
stated in section 2.1.2 and in this section, there are only three possible ways to allocate a flight: 
1. It can be allocated to chutes that belong to its own baggage handler (Aviator, Menzies or 
SGH) and are in a suitable area (red or blue if it is a flight handled with containers, and 
green or blue if it is a flight handled with belly wagons). 
2. It can be allocated to the flexible chutes (they are suitable for belly wagons and containers). 
3. It can be allocated both to chutes that belong to its own baggage handler and are in a 
suitable area, and to chutes that are flexible (if these two types of chutes are adjacent). 
Figure 4. The two types of loading facilities at CPH Airport. 
Left: container (Schinzel, 2009). Right: belly wagon (Omega Aviation, n.d.). 
==PAGE:18==
8 
 
2.2 RECAPITULATION OF THE PROBLEM AND THE ASSOCIATED CONSTRAINTS 
The research question of this Master Thesis is: “To what extent can the organization at BF3 
be optimized?”. To be more specific, CPH Airport has three main objectives with this project. 
First of all, one of the objectives is to determine the optimal number of flexible chutes necessary 
in BF3 in order to solve the CAP (there are currently 8 flexible chutes in BF3, see Figure 3). 
Secondly, another objective for CPH Airport is to be able to assess whether there is / are more 
adapted layout for BF3 with 8 flexible chutes (compared to the original layout of BF3). Finally, 
the last objective for CPH Airport is to determine whether a layout with less than 8 flexible 
chutes is a viable option for BF3. 
In order to answer to the research question, the CAP has to be solved, meaning that a 
mathematical model that schedules the assignment of the chutes in BF3 has to be 
implemented. Given the data provided by CPH Airport, the chute assignment schedules have 
to be generated on a daily basis with time intervals of 5 minutes. Furthermore, according to 
the description of BF3 made in section 2.1, the CAP is subjected to three main sets of 
constraints that are recapitulated below. 
1. Respect of the 1st repartition: 
The assignment of the chutes in BF3 to the different flights has to respect the chutes’ type. 
More specifically, baggage handlers’ chutes can only be assigned to the airlines they each 
service, and flexible chutes can only be used by the baggage handlers when none of their 
chutes is available. 
2. Respect of the 2nd repartition: 
The assignment of the chutes in BF3 to the different flights has to respect the areas’ type. 
More specifically, flights that are using containers to transport baggage can only be 
allocated to chutes located in the high and medium areas, and flights that are using belly 
wagons can only be allocated to chutes located in the medium and low areas. 
3. Respect of the adjacency principle: 
Every flight has its own demand in terms of chutes, between 0.5 and 3, see section 2.1.1. 
When more than one chute has to be allocated to one flight, the chutes have to be adjacent 
to each other. This means that these chutes also have to be located within the same 
workstation. A workstation is defined as a set of chutes located next to each other. Aisles 
and last minute chutes separate all the different workstations. For instance, chutes 33 to 
38, chutes 39 to 48, and chutes 49 to 51 define three distinct workstations in the same row 
of chutes, see Figure 3. As for the adjacency principle, let’s imagine an example where 
SAS, whose baggage are handled by SGH, has a flight that requires 3 chutes and 
containers to load the baggage. This flight would need to be allocated to the high (red) or 
==PAGE:19==
9 
 
medium (blue) area and to the chutes of SGH. However, the adjacency principle implies 
that the three required chutes could not be chutes 31, 32, and 33, or chutes 75, 76, and 
77. Indeed, between chutes 32 and 33 there is an aisle, and between chutes 76 and 77 
there is a last minute chute, see Figure 3. 
Several aspects are however not considered in this project. First of all, as mentioned above, 
last minute chutes and EBS are out of the scope of the project. Additionally, in real life, flights 
can be delayed or cancelled, and this is also not addressed in this project. Indeed, it is 
considered that, in case of flights delays or cancellations, the model could be run again with 
the updated information, and a new chute allocation schedule would be found. 
Moreover, it has to be said that, even if CPH Airport owns the chutes in BF3, on a daily basis, 
each baggage handling company is responsible for its use of its own chutes. In other words, 
CPH Airport is not part of the process of allocating the chutes to the different flights. However, 
as it was mentioned above, the airport is investigating every year which chutes need to be 
assigned to which baggage handler. This means that the airport is still responsible for the 
number of chutes that are allocated to the baggage handlers every year. It is therefore 
important for CPH Airport to be able to verify that the repartition of the chutes they make in 
BF3 is adapted to the chutes demand of each baggage handling company. A wrong repartition 
of the chutes between the baggage handlers would have drastic consequences in terms of 
flights delays. Consequently, solving the CAP in BF3 is also a way for CPH Airport to ensure 
that each baggage handling company has enough chutes to face its own baggage demand, 
and to verify that the three sets of constraints introduced above can be respected. 
Furthermore, it has to be mentioned that there are some days at CPH Airport where the traffic 
is so dense that it is not possible for the baggage handlers to allocate all of their flights while 
respecting the three sets of constraints introduced above. Since in real-life all the flights still 
have to be allocated, when this happens, the baggage handlers can reduce the chutes demand 
of their flights. To be more specific, flights with a demand of 3 chutes are allowed to use 2 
chutes only, and the opening time of the associated chutes can also be reduced. If necessary, 
flights with a demand of 2 chutes are also allowed to use 1 chute only. This “reduction process” 
is currently done by hand by the baggage handlers. All in all, possible un-allocations have to 
be taken into account when modelling the CAP, see Chapter 6. 
Finally, in agreement with CPH Airport, seven stages are addressed during this project in order 
to answer to the research question. These stages are presented below following their 
chronological development. 
Stage 1: Review of the literature available regarding airport baggage handling processes and 
adjacent resource scheduling, see Chapter 3. 
==PAGE:20==
10 
 
Stage 2: Analysis of the two datasets provided by CPH Airport, see Chapter 5. 
Stage 3: Creation of a first mathematical model (Mathematical Model 1, MM1) able to solve 
the CAP, see section 6.1. 
Stage 4: First approach to the research question with MM1, see section 7.1. 
- 
Determination of the optimal solutions of the CAP for the original layout of BF3, see 
section 7.1.1. 
- 
Determination of five more adapted layouts for BF3 by directly modifying the original 
layout of BF3, see section 7.1.2. 
- 
Determination of the optimal solutions in BF3 when all the chutes are considered 
flexible (i.e. can be shared by the baggage handlers), see section 7.1.3. 
Stage 5: Creation of a second mathematical model (Mathematical Model 2, MM2) able to solve 
the CAP, and to provide more information than MM1, see section 6.2. 
Stage 6: Second approach to the research question with MM2, see section 7.2. 
- 
Determination of the optimal solutions of the CAP for the original layout of BF3, see 
section 7.2.1. 
- 
Determination of four more adapted layouts for BF3 by directly modifying the original 
layout of BF3, see section 7.2.2. 
- 
Determination of the optimal solutions in BF3 when all the chutes are considered 
flexible and MM2 decides on the optimal split of the chutes between the baggage 
handlers, see section 7.2.3. 
Stage 7: Discussion of the findings and the limitations of the project, answer to the research 
question, see Chapter 8. 
 
The results of these seven stages are presented and analysed in the following chapters of the 
report. In Chapter 3, a review of literature is performed. 
 
 
==PAGE:21==
11 
 
3. LITERATURE REVIEW 
 
This chapter corresponds to the first stage of the project, as introduced in section 2.2. More 
specifically, this chapter investigates two different directions, literature specifically linked to 
airport baggage handling processes, and more general literature regarding the category of 
problems that the CAP is connected to, Adjacent Resource Scheduling (ARS) problems. 
First of all, when researching specifically on airport baggage handling processes, it can be 
seen that it has become extensively researched on since the 90’s. The following Figure 5 
displays the evolution of the number of publications involving “Airport Baggage Handling”, 
using DTU Findit. 
Figure 5. Evolution of the number of publications involving “Airport Baggage Handling” (DTU Findit, 2016). 
 
Looking at Figure 5, a logical correlation can be made with the fact that, before the 90’s, airports 
were significantly smaller, and airport operations problems were most likely solved using 
greedy heuristics. It can be assumed that the significant increase in passenger traffic over the 
past twenty years has made it necessary to find new algorithms to solve airport operation 
problems (West, 2011). 
In Abdelghany, 2006, the baggage handling process is addressed by considering a 
combination of piers placed after a main conveyor belt that carries all the passengers’ 
baggage. A pier is a long structure that extends from a central area of a building, the main 
conveyor belt in this case. In other words, piers can be seen as sorting stations for baggage. 
To be more specific, Abdelghany, 2006, considers that one, and only one, pier can be assigned 
per flight. To solve the airport baggage handling pier assignment Abdelghany, 2006, introduces 
a model that assigns the baggage of departing flights to available piers in the baggage handling 
facility. The baggage factory piers are considered as limited infrastructure resources that need 
to be assigned to a number of prescheduled flights. A selective algorithm is presented based 
10
28
34
27
192
532
174
0
100
200
300
400
500
600
1949-1959 1960-1969 1970-1979 1980-1989 1990-1999 2000-2009 2010-2014
AIRPORT BAGGAGE HANDLING
==PAGE:22==
12 
 
on a last-in-first-out queue discipline. In both Asco, 2012, and Asco, 2014, airport baggage 
handling is considered as a multi-objective and a multi-constraint problem. Asco, 2012, 
develops an evolutionary algorithm which assigns the different flights to different baggage-
sorting stations. The objective is both to minimize the number of flights that cannot be allocated 
to a sorting station, and to minimize the distance between the flights and the chosen sorting 
stations. Buffer times are also introduced to cope with uncertainties such as flight delays. In 
Asco, 2014, a similar approach is used. The problem also aims at minimizing the distance 
between the sorting stations and the flights, and a focus is made on ensuring a fair repartition 
of the workload. Different evolutionary algorithms are introduced and compared. 
Justesen, 2014, introduces all the different airport operations with a focus on ground handling 
resource allocation problems. The baggage handling process is assessed based on data 
provided by Frankfurt airport. A separation is made between in-bound, transfer and out-bound 
baggage handling, and three different models are presented based on the same general 
model. In-bound baggage handling, at Frankfurt airport, is handled through carousels. More 
specifically, carousels are huge circle-shaped conveyor systems. As for the piers, carousels 
can be seen as sorting stations for baggage, and each carousel is able to accommodate 
several flights. In Justesen, 2014, the aim is to assign flights to baggage carousels, and the 
objectives are a combination of minimizing direct costs, and balancing the workload, in a similar 
manner as in Asco, 2014. 
Frey, 2010, models the baggage handling process as a resource-constrained project 
scheduling problem. Baggage handling process is addressed by considering a combination of 
circle-shaped conveyor belts called circulations. Several issues are shaping the problem such 
as which circulation should be assigned to which flight, or at what time should a circulation be 
opened to process the baggage of a flight. The problem is demonstrated to be NP-hard, and a 
decomposition into a generalized assignment problem is proposed. 
From all the above publications, it appears that baggage handling processes are intrinsically 
connected to the layout of the baggage factory facilities considered. For instance, flights can 
be assigned to a unique baggage sorting station (Abdelghany, 2006, Asco, 2012, and Asco, 
2014), or several flights can be allocated to the same baggage sorting station (Justesen, 2014). 
And, the baggage sorting stations are different for each airport, for instance they can consist 
of a combination of piers (e.g. at Gatwick Airport), carousels (e.g. at Frankfurt Airport), or 
chutes (e.g. at CPH Airport). Additionally, it is noted that the complexity of baggage handling 
assignment problems is NP-hard (Frey, 2010). 
Besides, compared to the above publications, because of the use of chutes, additional layout 
constraints are shaping the CAP at CPH Airport. Indeed, Chapter 2 showed that three different 
==PAGE:23==
13 
 
baggage handling companies are working in BF3, and that they each use two different types 
of loading facilities (containers and belly wagons). All this led to a very specific repartition of 
the chutes in BF3 with different zones for each baggage handler. Furthermore, adjacency 
constraints have to be taken into account when flights have a demand of more than one chute. 
As a result, it appears that publications regarding “airport baggage handling” are insufficient to 
describe the CAP at CPH Airport. Therefore, additional research regarding the category of 
problems that the CAP is connected to were made and are presented in the following. 
From a general view, the CAP falls under the category of resource scheduling where resources 
(flights) are allocated to activities (chutes) over time. More specifically, the use of chutes is 
limited and constrained in various ways, as it was defined in Chapter 2, which makes the CAP 
a constraint-based scheduling problem. Constraint-based scheduling has become the 
dominant form of modelling and solving scheduling problems (Fromherz, 2001). Fromherz, 
2001, gives an overview of different types of constraint scheduling problems. The different 
operation constraints that govern each problem differ to great extent, and a suitable modelling 
approach is introduced in each case. The NP-completeness of these types of problems is also 
addressed. 
Resource and constraint-based scheduling however are not sufficient to describe the CAP. 
Due to the fact that up-to three chutes can be allocated to one flight, and that these chutes 
have to be adjacent, the CAP falls under the category of Adjacent Resource Scheduling (ARS). 
ARS is a rather new topic, and it has been extensively researched on since the 90’s. The 
following Figure 6 displays the evolution of the number of publications involving “Adjacent 
Resource Scheduling”, using DTU Findit. 
Figure 6. Evolution of the number of publications involving “Adjacent Resource Scheduling” (DTU Findit; 2016). 
 
 
0
0
3
5
66
206
224
0
50
100
150
200
250
1949-1959
1960-1969
1970-1979
1980-1989
1990-1999
2000-2009
2010-2014
ADJACENT RESOURCE SCHEDULING
==PAGE:24==
14 
 
The evolution presented in Figure 6 can be logically linked to the fact that in the 90’s it has 
become more possible to run mathematical ARS models thanks to the use of more powerful 
embedded processors (Fromherz, 2001). However, when researching on ARS problems linked 
to airport baggage handling processes, little literature is found. Indeed, as mentioned above, 
the adjacency principle is a specificity of CPH Airport baggage factory BF3. 
The adjacency principle however can be found in other airport processes such as the check-
in counter allocation. This problem is a classical ARS application where the aim is to assign a 
number of adjacent desks for a fixed period of time for each considered flight (Duin, 2006). 
Modified constraint-based scheduling algorithms need to be produced in order to incorporate 
the adjacency principle (Chun, 1996). A block representation of the check-in counters is used 
in Chun, 1996, where scheduling requirements are modelled as multi-dimensional objects. 
More specifically, the check-in counters are treated as multi-dimensional objects that need to 
be moulded into unallocated free space until a place is found for each of these objects. Given 
the size of the problems, Gantt charts are introduced to visually display the results of the 
algorithms. The same block representation is used in Duin, 2006. Each counter is not 
considered separately but several counters are considered as one block. One block is 
assigned per flight, and all the check-in counter blocks need to be fit with each other. An 
emphasis is made on the fact that adjacency requirements in resource-constrained problems 
is unnatural when using computing applications, and that these models are strongly NP-
complete. The check-in counter allocation is linked to known problems of processor 
scheduling, and two dimension placements (Duin, 2006). 
In Yan, 2004, check-in counters are also considered as blocks, and blocking plans are 
designed according to the number of service lines. However, various factors influence the 
creation of the blocks such as the number of passengers, or the personnel available for a 
specific flight. The fact that the process is stochastic is introduced, and three different heuristic 
models are presented. The resource requirement is also considered as stochastic in Chun, 
1996. The arrival of passengers is described as a non-stationary probability distribution, and 
the focus is made on the fact that the available queuing space for passengers differs for each 
check-in counter. Simulation techniques can be combined with constraint-based scheduling 
when creating the blocks to handle the stochastic nature of the process (Chun, 1996). 
For the same reasons described in Chun, 1996, baggage handling at airport is a stochastic 
process since the number of checked-in bags has a non-stationary probability distribution. 
However, for this project, the baggage handling process at BF3 is considered deterministic. 
Indeed, CPH Airport provided two datasets that both include the chutes demand of each 
considered flight, as introduced in section 2.1.1. 
==PAGE:25==
15 
 
Finally, several prior projects were made at CPH Airport concerning BF3 (Axelsen, 2013 and 
Hafþórsdóttir, 2014). In Axelsen, 2013, the influence of extending the opening times of chutes 
was investigated. And, in Hafþórsdóttir, 2014, the aim was to find an optimal allocation of the 
chutes that respects the habits of the baggage handlers. In the latter, 61 chutes were 
considered to be flexible. The adjacency principle was determined to make the problem 
strongly NP-complete (Hafþórsdóttir, 2014). In both cases, the problem was considered to be 
deterministic, and block representations, similarly as in Chun, 1996, Yan, 2004, and Duin, 
2006, were used to model the allocation of the different chutes. Lastly, these two projects 
respectively occurred in 2013 and 2014, and since then BF3 experienced several operational 
changes. Among others, CPH Airport is now working with a new baggage handling company, 
Menzies, and only 8 chutes are currently considered to be flexible, see Chapter 2. As a result, 
CPH Airport currently aims at including these changes in order to solve the CAP again and 
optimize the usage of BF3. 
To conclude, given the NP complexity of the problem at hand, the use of block representations, 
as introduced in Chun, 1996, Yan, 2004, and Duin, 2006, to model the CAP is investigated 
further in Chapter 4. 
 
 
==PAGE:26==
16 
 
4. METHODOLOGY 
 
This Chapter presents the methodology followed in order to create mathematical models able 
to solve the CAP. 
First of all, as explained in Chapter 3, baggage handling optimization problems are intrinsically 
linked to the layout of the baggage factory. Given what was explained in Chapter 2, the CAP 
is submitted to three main sets of constraints: the respect of the 1st repartition (type of the 
chutes, i.e. baggage handlers’ chutes and flexible chutes), the respect of the 2nd repartition 
(type of the areas, i.e. low, medium and high), and the respect of the adjacency principle (when 
a flight requires more than one chute). All these aspects make the CAP an Adjacent Resource 
Scheduling (ARS) Problem. 
Additionally, as detailed in Chapter 3, the complexity of ARS problems is NP-hard. This reason 
has made it necessary to research on mathematical models able to handle adjacency 
requirements. It appeared that modified constraint-based scheduling algorithms are able to 
incorporate the adjacency principle (Chun, 1996, Yan, 2004, and Duin, 2006). To do so, block 
representation, where scheduling requirements are modelled as multi-dimensional objects, 
can be used. This method was also successfully used by CPH Airport during two prior projects 
(Axelsen, 2013 and Hafþórsdóttir, 2014). 
Based on all the above, it was therefore decided to use block representations (i.e. multi-
dimensional objects) in order to solve the CAP. However, it has to be said that a very limited 
amount of articles and publications was found regarding both ARS problems and airport 
baggage handling optimization, see Chapter 3. In other words, the use of two-dimensional 
objects to model the CAP is the only suitable method that was found during this project, which 
does not mean that there are no other better ways to actually solve the CAP. 
All in all, as it is detailed in Chapter 6, two-dimensional objects are generated for each flight. 
More specifically, these objects comprise each flight’s information regarding the chutes 
demand, and the associated opening time of the chute(s). By doing so, the three sets of 
constraints of the CAP are directly handled in the generation of the two-dimensional objects, 
which is a way to reduce the complexity of the mathematical models used to solve the CAP. 
In other words, for each flight, two-dimensional objects are only generated if they respect the 
adjacency principle, the loading facility, and the baggage handling company that the flight 
needs to use. 
 
 
==PAGE:27==
17 
 
5. DATA ANALYSIS 
 
This chapter displays the second stage of the project, as introduced in section 2.2. In this 
chapter, the two datasets provided by CPH Airport are presented and analysed in order to see 
whether some prior conclusions can be drawn from them. 
5.1 
OVERVIEW OF THE TWO DATASETS 
First of all, CPH Airport provided two different datasets: 
- 
Set 1: Four days of different traffic profiles spread across 2014 (CPH Airport [6], 2016). 
- 
Set 2: Seven days of April 2014 (CPH Airport [7], 2016). 
To be more specific, in the two datasets, all the flights’ information of a given day can be found: 
STD, airline, flight number, destination, baggage handler, chutes demand profile (i.e. chutes 
demand and associated opening time of the chute(s)), and loading facility (containers or belly 
wagons). It has to be said that, in Set 1, it is the theoretical chutes demand profile that is given 
for each flight, whereas, in Set 2, it is the actual information on how many chutes were given 
to the flights by the baggage handling companies, and for how long, which can be denoted as 
the actual chutes demand profile. 
Note that the information of the two datasets are from 2014. CPH Airport explained that no 
major operational changes occurred in BF3 since 2014 regarding both the organization of the 
chutes and the amount of bags handled by the baggage handling companies. Therefore, it is 
considered that these two datasets are still representative of the baggage handling process in 
BF3, and that it is not an issue that the data are two years old. 
It was agreed with CPH Airport that Set 1 would be the main focus of the project. Indeed, Set 
1 provides a good overview of the traffic experienced at CPH Airport, in terms of number of 
flights and demand distribution of the baggage handling process. Additionally, it is also 
considered that Set 1 is more reliable than Set 2, since the theoretical chutes demand are 
given. Therefore, it was agreed with CPH Airport that the CAP would be solved based on the 
data of Set 1, and that Set 2 would only be used as a back-up set. 
Moreover, an adjustment had to be made on the two datasets because they comprised 
information for both baggage factories BF2 and BF3. The flights handled in BF2 are all handled 
by the baggage handler CFS, and these flights were therefore removed from both datasets. 
The information about BF3, for the two datasets, are presented in the following Table 1.

of all the flights during the day, and more specifically based on how many peaks in the chutes 
demand there are for the baggage handling companies. Therefore, the more the baggage 
handling companies are expected to use the flexible chutes during the day, the more this day 
is critical. Finally, in Set 1, it can be seen that May 14th 2014 is the day with the greatest number 
of flights and is only rated as Medium, which shows that the traffic types are not dependent of 
the amount of flights during the days.

From Table 2, it can be seen that the information of the two datasets are consistent with each 
other (the percentages of the two sets are comparable). In average, both Menzies and SGH 
mainly handle flights with a demand of 1 chute, whereas Aviator mainly handles flights with a 
demand of 2 chutes. And, in average, SGH handles the biggest proportion of the flights in BF3, 
followed by Aviator, and by Menzies. Furthermore, even if in average there are more flights in 
Set 2 than in Set 1, the total chutes demand of Set 1 is greater than the one of Set 2. More 
specifically, in Set 1, there is an average demand of 1.53 chutes per flight, whereas in Set 2 
the average demand is of 1.36 chutes per flight. In other words, it tends to show that Set 1 
might be more challenging for BF3 than Set 2. This remark is discussed further in Chapter 8. 
Since Set 1 is the focus of the project, a comprehensive analysis of this set is made in the 
following sections. To simplify, in the rest of the report, each day of Set 1 is referred to as its 
own traffic type (i.e. Low, Medium, Busy and Very Busy). Firstly, in section 5.2, the number of 
chutes assigned to each baggage handler in each of the different areas is compared to the 
amount of flights of each loading type (containers and belly wagons) that each baggage 
handler services. Then, in section 5.3, an analysis of the chutes demands is performed for 
each of the four days of Set 1.

The above Table 3 and Table 4 can be used in order to determine how the baggage handlers 
generally use their blue chutes (suitable for both containers and belly wagons). In other words, 
it is possible to use the two tables in order to approximate how many chutes in total each 
baggage handler generally uses for flights handled with containers and for flights handled with 
belly wagons, see the following. 
Looking at the above Table 4, it can be seen that approximately 90% of Menzies’ flights are 
belly wagon flights. Also taking into account Table 3, since Menzies is given 6 green chutes, 4 
blue chutes, and no red chute, it can be considered that in general 9 of its chutes (6 green 
chutes and 3 blue chutes) are used for flights handled with belly wagons and that the remaining 
blue chute is used for flights handled with containers. Indeed, this repartition is consistent with 
the percentages given in Table 4. As for Aviator, it can be seen in Table 4 that approximately 
50% of its flights are handled with container. Given that Aviator is not given any red chutes, 
and that it is roughly given the same amount of green and blue chutes, it makes sense to 
consider that all of its blue chutes are generally used for flights handled with containers. Finally, 
SGH also has a balanced repartition of flights between belly wagon and container, see Table 
4. It can therefore be considered that in general half of its chutes are used for flights handled 
with containers and the other half for flights handled with belly wagons. Therefore, this leads 
to the idea that 36 chutes (6 green chutes and 30 blue chutes) are generally used for flights

handled with belly wagons and that the remaining 37 chutes (19 red chutes and 18 blue chutes)

The percentages found in Table 4 and Table 5 can be compared. For all three baggage 
handlers, the average chutes’ repartition per flights’ type is comparable with the flight’s 
repartition between container and belly wagon flights since the percentages are similar. 
Looking specifically into each baggage handler, Aviator usually handles more container flights 
(53%) whereas 56% of its chutes are generally used for flights handled with belly wagons. As 
for SGH, it usually handles more belly wagon flights (55%) whereas 51% of its chutes are 
generally used for container flights. Given the total amount of flights that have to be handled 
every day at BF3 by SGH and Aviator, these differences, even if they seem rather small, can 
have a critical impact on the flights’ allocations. Indeed, a wrong repartition of the chutes, as 
mentioned in Chapter 2, can create drastic flight delays. Lastly, regarding Menzies, the 
percentages seem to be consistent since in general 89% of its flights are belly wagon flights, 
and 90% of its chutes are usually used for flights handled with belly wagons. 
All in all, it seems that the repartition of the chutes in the different areas is questionable for 
both Aviator and SGH. More specifically, blue chutes are the most convenient type of chutes 
for the baggage handlers since they are able to accommodate both belly wagons and 
containers. However, it seems that Aviator is given too many green chutes (10), compared to 
its amount of blue chutes (8), and that SGH is given too little green chutes (6), compared to its 
amount of red and blue chutes (respectively 19 and 48). Additionally, SGH is given a very small 
amount of green chutes (6) compared to its number of blue chutes (48) which is also 
questionable since it diminishes the amount of blue chutes available for Aviator and Menzies. 
To conclude, several comments can be made on these calculations. First of all, as explained 
above, the general usage of the blue chutes for each baggage handler is only an 
approximation. Indeed, each blue chute can accommodate both containers and belly wagons, 
and, for calculation purposes, it was considered that each blue chute can accommodate one 
loading type only (containers or belly wagons). These calculations were performed in order to 
have an idea on how the blue chutes are generally used in BF3. Therefore, all the above 
conclusions will be discussed in Chapter 7. Lastly, all the calculations are based on Set 1, and 
it was verified that the results remain comparable when Set 2 is also taken into account. 
==PAGE:32==
22 
 
5.3 
ANALYSIS OF THE CHUTES DEMAND IN BF3 
In this section, the chutes demands of Set 1 are analysed. To simplify, in this section, the 
amount of chutes that is given to each of the baggage handlers is referred to as their capacity, 
the sum of the chutes given to the baggage handlers is referred to as the total capacity of BF3, 
and the sum of the chutes given to the baggage handlers plus the flexible chutes is referred to 
as the maximum capacity of BF3. Therefore the capacity of Aviator is 18 chutes, the capacity 
of Menzies is 10 chutes, the capacity of SGH is 73 chutes, the total capacity of BF3 is 101 
chutes, and the maximum capacity of BF3 is 109 chutes, see Figure 3. In section 5.3.1, for 
each day of Set 1, a general overview of the chutes demand is given for the three baggage 
handlers, using five minute intervals, and is compared to both the total capacity of BF3 and the 
maximum capacity of BF3. Finally, in section 5.3.2, for each day of Set 1, an overview of the 
chutes demand is given for each baggage handler and is compared to its own capacity. 
Several remarks have to be made regarding the limitations of the following analysis. First of 
all, it was mentioned in Chapter 2 that domestic flights have a demand of 0.5 chute, meaning 
that, when possible, these flights are paired. However, in reality at CPH Airport, all domestic 
flights cannot be paired, and, in that case, these flights are left with a demand of 1 chute. In 
the following analysis, all domestic fights are considered to have a demand of 0.5 chute, which 
is too optimistic compared to what really happens in BF3. The process of pairing domestic 
flights is actually addressed in section 7.1.1. Additionally, the following analysis only takes into 
account one of the three sets of constraints introduced in section 2.2: the 1st repartition 
(according to the type of chutes, i.e. baggage handlers’ chutes and flexible chutes). In other 
words, in the following analysis, a baggage handler might have enough chutes in theory to 
allocate all of its respective flights at a specific time, however since the 2nd repartition 
(according to the type of area, i.e. containers and belly wagons) is not taken into account, in 
real life, the baggage handler might need to use the flexible chutes or might even end up with 
unallocated flights. The same remark can be made for the last set of constraints of the CAP, 
the adjacency principle. In the following analysis, a baggage handler might have enough 
chutes in theory to allocate all of its respective flights, however, because of the layout of the 
different workstations, in real life, the adjacency principle might force the baggage handlers to 
use the flexible chutes, or might even be responsible for unallocated flights in the optimal 
solutions. All in all, since two sets of constraints are dropped in this analysis, the optimal 
solutions of the CAP can only be worse than the results that are displayed in this section. This 
remark is discussed further in Chapter 8. 
 
==PAGE:33==
23 
 
5.3.1 GENERAL OVERVIEW OF THE CHUTES DEMAND IN BF3 
In this section, for each day of Set 1, the distribution of the chutes demand along the day for 
all three baggage handling companies is presented in Figure 7 below. In each case, the chutes 
demand is compared to the total capacity of BF3 and to the maximum capacity of BF3. 
 
From the above Figure 7, it can be seen that none of the four days has a chutes demand that 
exceeds the maximum capacity of BF3. Additionally, even if there are times where the chutes 
demand is close to the total capacity of BF3 (see Busy and Very Busy in Figure 7), the total 
capacity of BF3 is also never exceeded. However, due to the remarks made at the beginning 
of section 5.3, it is most likely that the flexible chutes will still be used in the 4 days, and that 
un-allocations might also occur. To conclude, it can be said that Figure 7 is insufficient to 
determine any prior results regarding the flexible chutes usage and possible un-allocations in

5.3.2 OVERVIEW OF THE CHUTES DEMAND IN BF3 FOR EACH BAGGAGE HANDLER 
In this section, for each day of Set 1, the chutes demand of each baggage handling company 
is compared to its capacity and to its maximum capacity (capacity plus the flexible chutes).

From the above graphs, it can be seen that none of the baggage handlers is exceeding its own 
capacity in Low. Going further, it can actually be said that, during the whole day, each baggage 
handler is generally far from reaching its capacity, and that there are no peaks in the chutes 
demand. For these reasons, it is verified that the traffic is low in this specific day. In conclusion, 
it can be predicted that the use of flexible chutes will be very limited on this day, and that all 
the flights should be allocated to the chutes.

From the above graphs, it can be seen that only Menzies has a demand exceeding its capacity 
during the day, with a peak at 14:50. Therefore, in theory, only Menzies needs to use the 
flexible chutes in this day. However, for the reasons mentioned at the beginning of section 5.3, 
SGH and Aviator might also need to use the flexible chutes during this day. Additionally, the 
maximum chutes demand of Menzies is 18 chutes (at 14:50) which is equal to the maximum 
capacity of Menzies (10 chutes plus the 8 flexible chutes). It can already be said that, if SGH 
or Aviator need to use at least one of the flexible chutes at this same time, some flights will be 
unallocated. Finally, compared to the previous profile (Low), the traffic is denser since one of 
the baggage handlers (Menzies) is exceeding its capacity during the day.

From the above graphs, it can be seen that at some point during the day, all the baggage 
handlers are exceeding their own capacity. More specifically, the critical time period of Aviator 
is from 7:40 to 9:35, with a maximum demand of 22 chutes (4 more than its capacity). 
Moreover, during the whole day, Menzies has a chutes demand close to its capacity. Two 
critical periods stand out, from 8:50 to 9:40 and from 11:15 to 16:35. During these periods, the 
chutes demand oscillates between 7 and 14 chutes. Finally, SGH only experience one critical 
moment from 6:10 to 6:35 with a maximum demand of 78 chutes (5 more than its capacity). 
Additionally, it can be said that the critical periods of all the baggage handlers do not overlap, 
which means that, in theory, they will not be using the flexible chutes at the same time. It can 
also be seen that, for each baggage handler, the amount of chutes that exceeds its capacity 
at any time during the day is always smaller than its maximum capacity, meaning that all the 
flights might be able to be allocated to the chutes during this day. However, for the reasons 
mentioned at the beginning of section 5.3, un-allocations are still likely to occur during this day. 
Lastly, even if less flights are handled compared to Medium, it is clear that the traffic in Busy 
is denser, since all the baggage handlers are exceeding their own capacity during the day. 
 
28.06.2014: Very busy traffic profile 
At CPH Airport, June 28th 2014 was rated as a very busy traffic day, and 305 flights were 
handled in total by the three baggage handlers in BF3. The distribution of the chutes demand 
along the day for each baggage handler is presented in Figure 11, in the following page. 
In Figure 11, it can be seen that the chutes demand of SGH is never exceeding its capacity. 
Around 6:00 however there is a peak in the chutes demand, and the numbers are actually 
really close to its capacity. Due to the reasons stated at the beginning of section 5.3, it is likely 
that SGH will use flexible chutes during this period. Moreover, both Aviator and Menzies are 
exceeding their own capacity. Two periods are critical for Aviator, from 8:50 to 9:30, and from 
10:50 to 14:10, with a maximum demand of 27 chutes at the same time. As for Menzies, the 
critical period is between 10:50 and 14:40 where the chutes demand oscillates between 8 and 
12 chutes. Furthermore, the maximum demand of chutes at a time for Aviator is 27 (from 12:25 
to 12:35), which is greater than its maximum capacity (26: 18 chutes plus 8 flexible chutes). 
Therefore, it can already be predicted that some flights will be unallocated during this period. 
This might even be critical if SGH or Menzies also need to use flexible chutes during this same 
time period. Finally, it can be said that this day is considered very busy due to the fact that the 
chutes demand is dense during most of the day for all baggage handlers, and because the 
chutes demand of Aviator is greater than its maximum capacity at some point during the day 
(from 12:25 to 12:35). 
==PAGE:38==
28 
 
 
To conclude on section 5.3.2, it was seen that, in Medium, only Menzies has a chutes demand 
that exceeds its capacity, in Busy, all the baggage handlers are actually exceeding their own 
capacity at some point during the day, and in Very Busy, both Aviator and Menzies are 
exceeding their own capacity during the day. However, among the four days, only Aviator 
exceeds its maximum capacity in Very Busy. In order to get a better overview of these results, 
the following Figure 12 displays the days, and the times, where the chutes demands of the 
baggage handlers are exceeding their capacity by representing these excesses and comparing 
them to the amount of flexible chutes available in BF3 (8).

demand of Aviator’s flights. Finally, it clearly appears in Figure 12 that there is a disproportion

Looking at Table 6, it can be said that in Medium at least 28% (6 hours and 45 minutes) of the 
day requires the use of the flexible chutes by the baggage handlers, at least 27% in Busy (6 
hours and 30 minutes), and at least 18% in Very Busy (4 hours and 15 minutes). Additionally, 
it can also be seen that among the three days, for the three baggage handlers, the average 
chutes demand above the total capacity of BF3 is of 2.2 chutes, and that the average maximum 
chutes demand above the total capacity of BF3 is of 7.2 chutes. In other words, for the above 
three days, the average chutes demand above the total capacity of BF3 seems reasonable, 
since 2.2 chutes corresponds to approximately a quarter of the 8 flexible chutes. However, the 
average maximum chutes demand above the total capacity of BF3 seems critical, since 7.2 
chutes is really close to the limit of 8 flexible chutes available. All in all, for the reasons stated 
==PAGE:41==
31 
 
at the beginning of section 5.3, it can already be foreseen that there will be more un-allocations 
that what could be predicted in this analysis (un-allocations can only be predicted in Very Busy 
for flights handled by Aviator) when the CAP will be solved, see Chapter 7. This remark is also 
discussed further is Chapter 8. 
 
To conclude, Chapter 5 pointed out that the current repartition of the chutes between the 
baggage handlers in BF3 is questionable. Indeed, section 5.2 highlighted that Aviator seems 
to have too many green chutes (10) compared to its amount of blue chutes (8), and that SGH 
seems to have too little green chutes (6) compared to its amount of red and blue chutes 
(respectively 19 and 48). Additionally, it showed that SGH might have too many blue chutes 
(48) compared to the amount of blue chutes given to both Aviator (8) and Menzies (4). 
Furthermore, section 5.3 proved that, in theory, Aviator and Menzies need to use the flexible 
chutes more often than SGH. In other words, it might mean that SGH has too many chutes in 
total compared to both Aviator and Menzies. Section 5.3 also demonstrated that, in theory, the 
baggage handlers have enough chutes at their availability to allocate of all their flights, except 
for one period of time in Very Busy when the maximum capacity of BF3 is excessed, see Figure 
12. All these remarks are actually taken into account when analysing the results of the CAP in 
Chapter 7. Finally, it has to be mentioned again that the analysis of section 5.3 only takes into 
account one set of the three sets of constraints of the CAP introduced in section 2.2. Therefore, 
it has to be kept in mind that the optimal solutions of the CAP can only be worse than the 
results foreseen with this analysis. As mentioned above, this remark is discussed further is 
Chapter 8. 
In the following Chapter 6, two different mathematical models able to solve the CAP are 
detailed. 
 
 
==PAGE:42==
32 
 
6. CHUTE ALLOCATION MODELS 
 
This chapter corresponds to the third and the fifth stages that were introduced in section 2.2. 
In this chapter, two different mathematical models that are able to solve the CAP are 
introduced. In each case the mathematical models are based on two-dimensional objects, as 
mentioned in Chapter 4. The generation process of the two-dimensional objects is also 
detailed, and the associated complexity of each mathematical model is addressed. 
6.1 
MATHEMATICAL MODEL 1: REWARD PROCESS BASED 
The Mathematical Model 1, MM1, introduced in this chapter is based on a reward process, as 
it is detailed in the following. To facilitate the comprehension, the generation of the two-
dimensional objects is detailed before presenting the mathematical model. 
6.1.1 GENERATION OF THE TWO-DIMENSIONAL OBJECTS 
As mentioned in Chapter 4, two-dimensional objects are generated for each flight. More 
specifically, the two dimensions are the chutes and the time, and each object only represents 
one flight. Additionally, for each flight, objects are only generated if they respect the constraints 
introduced in section 2.2: the chutes’ type, the areas’ type and the adjacency principle. In the 
following, the generation process of the objects is detailed, and a partial algorithm is given. 
First of all, to make it easier, each day is decomposed into 5 minute intervals, see Table 7. 
Table 7. Time management. 
Time periods 
Actual time 
0 
00:00 
1 
00:05 
2 
00:10 
… 
… 
286 
23:50 
287 
23:55 
 
Additionally, as mentioned in section 2.1.1, there are 4 different chute profiles and 2 different 
time profiles possible for each flight, see the following. 
Chute profiles: 
 Wide-body flights: 3 chutes. 
 Narrow-body flights (route w/ transfer): 2 chutes. 
 Narrow-body flights (route w/o transfer): 1 chute. 
 Domestic flights: 0.5 chute when possible, 1 chute 
otherwise. 
Time profiles: 
 Wide-body flights: chutes open 3.5 hours 
and close 20 minutes before STD (3h10 in 
total: 39 time periods). 
 Narrow-body and domestic flights: chutes 
open 2.5 hours and close 20 minutes before 
STD (2h10 in total: 27 time periods). 
==PAGE:43==
33 
 
Note that in the rest of the report, Time Period is denoted by TP. From the above information, 
it can be deduced that there are only 3 types of objects that can be generated 
Type 1 – Wide-body flights: 3 chutes for 39 time periods (TPs) 
Type 2 – Narrow body flights (route w/ transfer): 2 chutes for 27 TPs 
Type 3 – Narrow body flights (route w/o transfer) & domestic flights: 1 chute for 27 TPs 
To give an example, the first flight of Low, in Set 1, is considered. This flight is a narrow body 
flight with a demand of 2 chutes, is handled by Aviator, is using belly wagons, and its STD is 
at 06:00 (TP 72). In other words, this flight needs 2 chutes (Type 2), starting from TP 42, for 
27 TPs, in the blue and / or green area that belong to Aviator and / or are flexible chutes. When 
looking into the layout of BF3 introduced in section 2.1.1, see Figure 3, possible two-
dimensional objects are: 
- 
Chutes 46 and 47, from TP 42 to TP 68. 
- 
Chutes 47 and 48, from TP 42 to TP 68. 
- 
Chutes 49 and 50, from TP 42 to TP 68. 
In total, 20 different objects can be created for this specific flight. The same process is followed 
for each flight in order to generate all the appropriate two-dimensional objects. 
To be more specific, the algorithm that was implemented in Java in order to create the two-
dimensional objects is displayed in the following page. It can already be said that there are 
three main outputs (matrices) of this algorithm: 
 𝐴𝑜𝑓: a binary matrix that tells if object o is suitable for flight f. 
 𝐵𝑜𝑐𝑡: a binary matrix that tells if object o uses chute c at time t. 
 𝑅𝑓𝑐: a matrix that gives, for each flight f, the reward of using chute c. 
These three matrices are then used in MM1 as it is detailed in the following section. To be 
more accurate, the two matrices 𝐴𝑜𝑓 and 𝐵𝑜𝑐𝑡 store all the information regarding each two-
dimensional object. As for the 𝑅𝑓𝑐 matrix, it is the core of MM1 since it is a reward process 
based mathematical model. Indeed, 𝑅𝑓𝑐 is created because the baggage handling companies 
are required to use their own chutes before using the flexible chutes. 
To give more information on how the 𝑅𝑓𝑐 matrix is created for each flight, it can be said that 
when a flight is allocated to chutes in BF3, a reward is given for each chute that is used. Since, 
at CPH Airport, flights can have a demand of up to 3 chutes, see section 2.1.1, up to 3 rewards 
can be given per flight. Depending on where the flight is actually allocated, four different types 
of reward can be given to a baggage handler: 
1. ∝1: Reward when it uses one of its own chutes to allocate a flight. 
2. ∝2: Reward when it uses the closest flexible chute to its own chutes to allocate a flight. 
==PAGE:44==
34 
 
3. ∝3: Reward when it uses the 2nd closest flexible chute to its own chutes to allocate a flight. 
4. ∝4: Reward when it uses another of the flexible chutes to allocate a flight. 
For logical reasons, the coefficients were chosen in order to ensure: ∝1> ∝2> ∝3> ∝4. 
Furthermore, depending on the three baggage handlers (Aviator, Menzies and SGH) and the 
two loading facilities (belly wagons and containers), six different reward profiles can be created. 
Therefore, for each flight, it is its reward profile that determines how the above coefficients are 
arranged in its associated reward matrix 𝑅𝑓 𝑐. For more information, the six reward profiles are 
detailed in Appendix A. The algorithm implemented in Java is detailed in Table 8 below. 
Table 8. Algorithm implemented in Java that generates the two-dimensional objects of MM1.

6.1.2 MATHEMATICAL MODEL 1 
In this section, the first mathematical model, MM1, used to solve the CAP is introduced. The 
model is based on the three different matrices, generated with Java, that were presented in 
the previous section. The aim of MM1 is to allocate flights to the chutes while respecting all the 
constraints introduced in Chapter 2 and ensuring that a maximum of flights are allocated in the 
optimal solutions. Table 9 below presents the different notations of MM1.

First of all, some remarks can be made regarding the way the rewards 𝑅𝑜 are calculated. As 
mentioned in section 6.1.1, each object is created specifically for one flight, meaning that each 
reward 𝑅𝑜 is only associated to one specific flight. Additionally, for each flight, depending on 
its reward profile (i.e. depending on its baggage handler and the loading facility that is needed), 
there is a specific reward associated to the use of each chute given by the 𝑅𝑓𝑐 matrix, see 
section 6.1.1. For instance, given an object o suitable for flight f, and using chutes 𝑐1, 𝑐2, and 
𝑐3. 𝑅𝑜 is the sum of the rewards of using these specific chutes: 𝑅𝑜= 𝑅𝑓 𝑐1 + 𝑅𝑓 𝑐2 + 𝑅𝑓 𝑐3. 
Indeed, all the other terms are 0. 
Equation [1] gives the objective of MM1, which is to maximize the rewards (of using the 
baggage handlers’ chutes before using the flexible chutes) while minimizing the number of 
unallocated flights in each day. Indeed, as it was mentioned in Chapter 2, there are days at 
CPH Airport where the traffic is so dense that all the flights cannot be allocated to the chutes 
and respect all the constraints of the CAP introduced in section 2.2. When this happens, the 
baggage handling companies are allowed to reduce the chute demands of their flights (mainly 
of flights with a demand of 3 chutes) in order to free some chutes and thus enable the allocation 
of all the flights. This process is done by hand by the baggage handling companies, on a daily 
basis. Therefore, in agreement with CPH Airport, it was decided that the main objective of MM1 
was to allocate a maximum of flights to the chutes while respecting all the layout constraints 
of BF3, which explains why un-allocations are possible in this model. Furthermore, there is a 
penalty associated to the un-allocation of flights. This penalty was chosen to be: 𝜆= 2, in order 
to balance the greatest possible value of the reward given when using a chute. This value 
corresponds to the biggest possible coefficient of the 𝑅𝑓𝑐 matrix, which is given by 𝛼1 = 2 as 
explained in Appendix A. 
Constraints [2] and [3] are the allocation constraints. These constraints encompass all the 
layout requirements of BF3. More specifically, constraint [2] ensures that, for each flight, if it is 
allocated in the optimal solution, there is a unique suitable object used in the optimal solution. 
And, constraint [3] ensures that, for every chute and every time period, there is at most one 
object used in the optimal solution that requires to use the given chute at the given time period. 
Finally, constraints [4] and [5] ensure that the variables used in the model are binary ones. 
 
 
==PAGE:47==
37 
 
6.1.3 DESCRIPTION OF THE PROCESS 
First of all, all the computations were performed on the same computer with the following 
characteristics: 2.7 GHz Intel Core i5 processors and RAM of 4GB. 
When solving the CAP with MM1, four different steps occur: 
1. First of all, for each day considered, for all the corresponding flights, all the suitable 
two-dimensional objects are generated with Java. Three different matrices are 
generated, as detailed in section 6.1.1. 
2. MM1 is implemented in GAMS, according to section 6.1.2, and the three above 
matrices are imported. For each day, the optimal solution is found, and the ID of each 
object used in the optimal solution is given, along with the ID of all the flights 
unallocated, if there are any. 
3. The ID of the objects used in the optimal solution are then exported to Java. These 
data are used to generate the necessary information to create Gantt charts, as it is 
detailed in the following. 
4. For each day, five different Gantt charts are generated with GAMS. One chart is 
generated for both Aviator and Menzies, two charts are generated for SGH (because 
they handle a bigger proportion of the chutes), and one chart is generated for the 
flexible chutes. Each Gantt chart is a representation of the chutes in function of the 
time, and all the flights are represented in the charts with bars whose length 
corresponds to the time profile of the corresponding flight. The use of Gantt charts is a 
way to visualize what happens in the chutes during the days, especially for the flexible 
chutes. 
For more details, all the codes corresponding to MM1, implemented in both Java and GAMS, 
can be found in Appendix B.1. 
 
6.1.4 CONCLUSION ON MATHEMATICAL MODEL 1 
MM1 was created as a first step in the solving process of the CAP. Indeed, this model is able 
to give a good overview of what happens in BF3 during the different days. More specifically, 
this model is able to display whether any of the flights are unallocated during the days. 
However, there might be unused chutes during the different days, and this model is unable to 
give any information on the usage of the chutes by the three baggage handlers. Additionally, 
this model is unable to optimize the usage of the flexible chutes. Indeed, the flexible chutes 
can be used by any of the baggage handlers when none of their own chutes is available, and 
one of the aims of this project is to optimize their usage by minimizing their sharing between 
==PAGE:48==
38 
 
the different baggage handlers. Therefore, it was decided to use MM1 as a base in order to 
get an overview of the CAP and to see to what extent the problem is solvable. A second 
mathematical model, with a bigger complexity, was then created based on MM1. This model 
aims at minimizing the sharing of the flexible chutes, while still minimizing the number of 
unallocated flights. This second mathematical model is detailed in the following section. 
 
6.2 
MATHEMATICAL MODEL 2: FLEXIBLE CHUTES SHARING LIMITED 
In this section, a second mathematical model, MM2, is introduced. As for MM1, this model is 
based on two-dimensional objects. Again, the generation of the two-dimensional objects is 
detailed before presenting the mathematical model. 
6.2.1 GENERATION OF THE TWO-DIMENSIONAL OBJECTS 
A similar process to the one displayed in section 6.1.1 for MM1 is followed. More specifically, 
two-dimensional objects are generated for each flight only if they respect the constraints 
introduced in section 2.2: the chutes’ type, the areas’ type and the adjacency principle. Again, 
there are only three types of objects that can be generated, as explained in section 6.1.1. In 
the following, the generation process of the objects is detailed, and a partial algorithm is given. 
Compared to the algorithm displayed in section 6.1.1, there are five main outputs (matrices) in 
this new algorithm: 
 𝐴𝑜𝑓: a binary matrix that tells if object o is suitable for flight f. 
 𝐵𝑜𝑐𝑡: a binary matrix that tells if object o uses chute c at time t. 
 𝐶𝑜𝑐: a binary matrix that tells if object o uses chute c. 
 𝐷𝑖𝑓: a binary matrix that tells if flight f is handled by baggage handler i. 
 𝑃𝑐 𝑐′: a reward matrix that rewards the use of chutes that are adjacent. 
As for MM1, these five matrices are then used in MM2, as it is detailed in the following section. 
To be more accurate, the three matrices 𝐴𝑜𝑓, 𝐵𝑜𝑐𝑡 and 𝐶𝑜𝑐 store all the information regarding 
each two-dimensional object. As for the 𝐷𝑖𝑓 matrix, it is generated in order to control how many 
different baggage handlers are using each of the flexible chutes. Finally, the 𝑃𝑐 𝑐′ matrix is a 
reward matrix created in order to favour, for each baggage handler, the usage of the closest 
flexible chutes to their own chutes. To be more specific, this matrix is a simplified version of 
the reward matrix 𝑅𝑓𝑐 implemented in section 6.1.1, and is detailed in the following section. 
The algorithm implemented in Java in detailed in Table 10 below.

39

6.2.2 MATHEMATICAL MODEL 2 
In this section, the second mathematical model, MM2, used to solve the CAP is introduced. 
The model is based on the five different matrices that are generated with Java, and that were 
presented in the previous section. The aim of MM2 is to allocate flights to the chutes while 
respecting all the constraints introduced in Chapter 2, and both ensuring an optimized usage 
of the flexible chutes, and that a minimum of flights remain unallocated. Table 11 below 
presents the different notations of the model. 
Note that, in the following, when there is more than one baggage handler using a flexible chute 
during the day (i.e. 2 or 3 baggage handlers using the flexible chute), the additional baggage 
handlers are denoted as the extra baggage handlers (i.e.1 or 2 extra baggage handlers). 
 
Table 11. Notations of Mathematical Model 2.
