Equation [1] gives the objective of the model, and is composed of four different terms. In a 
similar manner as in MM1, the rewards 𝑃 𝑐 𝑐′ are maximized while the number of flights 
unallocated in the final solution are minimized. As for MM1, un-allocations are possible in this 
model since one of the aims of MM2 is to allocate a maximum of flights to the chutes while 
respecting all the layout constraints of BF3. As for the two last terms of the objective function, 
they are only relevant for the flexible chutes, and their aim is to minimize both the amount of 
baggage handlers using each chute (𝑣𝑖 𝑐) and the amount of extra baggage handlers using 
each chute (𝑤𝑐). The two sets of variables 𝑣𝑖 𝑐 and 𝑤𝑐 are actually overlapping, since they both 
minimize the amount of baggage handlers using each flexible chutes, however they are both 
necessary for the model to work. Indeed, without the 𝑤𝑐 variables, if several chutes were 
available, the model would be unable to make the difference between using only one chute 
with two (or three) baggage handlers working on it, and having two (or three) chutes with only 
one baggage handler working in each chute. With the 𝑤𝑐 variables, these two scenarios are 
not considered equal by MM2, and the second scenario is the preferred one. 
Regarding the reward parameters 𝑃 𝑐 𝑐′, it is based on the same principle as the rewards 𝑅𝑓 𝑐, 
introduced in section 6.1.1 with MM1. The baggage handlers are rewarded more when using 
their own chutes, before using the closest flexible chutes to their own chutes. This is done by 
combining two types of rewards: the rewards given when using adjacent chutes, that were 
inexistent in MM1, and the rewards given depending on the chutes’ type (one is given when 
the baggage handler is using its own chute, and one, smaller, is given if it is using the flexible 
chutes). The 𝑃 𝑐 𝑐′ parameters were introduced in MM2, instead of the 𝑅𝑓 𝑐 rewards, in order to 
diminish the complexity of the model. Indeed, 𝑃 𝑐 𝑐′ is based on the chutes set C, whereas 𝑅𝑓 𝑐 
is based on both the flights set F and the chutes set C, and their size are consequently different, 
see the following. 
|𝑃 𝑐 𝑐′| = 109 ∗109 = 11,881 
205 ∗109 ≤|𝑅𝑓 𝑐| ≤368 ∗109 ⇒22,345 ≤ |𝑅𝑓 𝑐| ≤40,112 
The values given to the different penalty parameters can also be addressed. The most 
important objective of the model is to allocate as many flights as possible, therefore the penalty 
of un-allocating a flight, given by 𝜆1, is the greatest one and was chosen to be 𝜆1 = 500. The 
two other penalties 𝜆2 and 𝜆3 were then chosen to be relatively small compared to 𝜆1. This way 
it is easier to know whether there are any unallocated flights in the optimal solution just by 
looking at the objective value. The two remaining penalties 𝜆2 and 𝜆3 are linked to each other, 
and 𝜆2 has to be greater than 𝜆3 since it is considered more important to minimize the amount 
of baggage handlers using each chute. Indeed, 𝜆3 was only introduced in order to 
counterbalance the greatest reward possible given by the 𝑃 𝑐 𝑐′ parameters (which is 2, see 
==PAGE:53==
43 
 
Table 11), otherwise the model would use as many chutes as possible in order to maximize 
the rewards of using adjacent chutes. By choosing 𝜆3 = 3, this scenario is avoided, and the 
total amount of chutes is minimized. Based on the chosen value for 𝜆3 and that 𝜆2 has to be 
greater than 𝜆3, it was chosen to have 𝜆2 = 10. To conclude on the values of the different 
penalties, it has to be said that they were arbitrarily chosen at first, and were then adjusted 
when solving the CAP in order to match all the different objectives. The challenge with MM2 is 
that it is a multi-objective problem, and when combining different penalty terms, it becomes 
impossible to control each objective separately. 
To conclude on Equation [1], the same comment as with MM1 can be made. In real-life in an 
airport, it is not possible to leave any flight un-allocated. Therefore, when this happens at CPH 
Airport, the baggage handling companies are reducing the chute demands of their flights 
(mainly of flights with a demand of 3 chutes) in order to free some chutes and thus enable the 
allocation of all the flights. This process is addressed in the following, see section 6.2.3. 
Constraints [2] and [3] are the allocation constraints, and are the exact same constraints that 
were introduced in MM1. These constraints encompass all the layout requirements of BF3. 
More specifically, constraint [2] ensures that, for each flight, if it is allocated in the optimal 
solution, there is a unique suitable object used in the optimal solution. And, constraint [3] 
ensures that, for every chute and every time period, there is at most one object used in the 
optimal solution that requires to use the given chute at the given time period. 
Constraints [4] to [11] are big M constraints, and detail how each new set of variables is 
activated. Constraints [4] and [5] are activating the 𝑣𝑖 𝑐 variables. More specifically, each chute 
and each handler are considered, if handler i is using chute c, then 𝑣𝑖 𝑐 becomes 1 (constraint 
[4]), otherwise it is set to 0 (constraint [5]). Constraints [6] to [9] are activating the 𝑝𝑐 and 𝑤𝑐 
variables. More specifically, each chute is considered, and if there is more than one baggage 
handler in chute c, then 𝑝𝑐 is set to 0 (constraint [6]) and 𝑤𝑐 counts how many extra baggage 
handler (1 or 2) are using the chute (constraint [7]), otherwise, if the chute is unused or if there 
is only one baggage handler in chute c, then 𝑝𝑐 is set to 1 (constraint [8]) and 𝑤𝑐 to 0 (constraint 
[9]). Constraints [10] and [11] are activating the 𝑞𝑖 𝑐 𝑐′ variables. These variables are set to be 
1 if both 𝑣𝑖 𝑐 and 𝑣𝑖 𝑐′ are 1. They are created to ensure that the model remain linear, and are 
used in the objective function in order to maximize the 𝑃 𝑐 𝑐′ rewards. Lastly, it can be said that 
three different big M notations were introduced (𝑀1, 𝑀2, and 𝑀3). For simplification purposes, 
all the big M values were chosen to be 100. These values could be tighter given that the values 
of the parameters and the variables are always smaller than 2. However, it was considered 
that this was out of the scope of the project, and the value of 100 was kept for all the three big 
M notations. 
==PAGE:54==
44 
 
Finally, constraints [12] to [17] give the possible values for each set of variables. More 
specifically, all the variables used in MM2 are binary ones, except for the 𝑤𝑐 variables that can 
take the values 0, 1 or 2. 
 
6.2.3 DESCRIPTION OF THE PROCESS 
A similar process to the one presented in section 6.1.3 is followed: 
1. For each day considered, for all the corresponding flights, all the suitable two-
dimensional objects are generated with Java. Five different matrices are generated, as 
detailed in section 6.2.1. 
2. MM2 is implemented in GAMS, according to section 6.2.2, and the five above matrices 
are imported. For each day, as in MM1, the optimal solution is found, and the ID of 
each object used in the optimal solution is given, along with the ID of all the flights 
unallocated, if there are any. Some new information can also be accessed. For each 
chute, the ID of the baggage handlers using it, if any, is given, which is a way to see 
whether there are some unused chutes in the optimal solution. Additionally, the ID of 
the chutes where there is more than one baggage handler in the final solution is also 
given, which is a way to directly see how many chutes are shared between the baggage 
handlers during the day. 
3. The ID of the objects used in the optimal solution are then exported to Java. These 
data are used to generate the necessary information to create Gantt charts, as it is 
detailed in the following. 
4. For each day, five different Gantt charts are generated with GAMS. One chart is 
generated for both Aviator and Menzies, two charts are generated for SGH (because 
they handle a bigger proportion of the chutes), and one chart is generated for the 
flexible chutes. 
Compared to section 6.1.3, two extra steps are added for days that have flights unallocated in 
their optimal solution. Indeed, as mentioned before, in real-life no un-allocations can remain in 
an optimal solution. Therefore, in case of un-allocations, MM2 is slightly modified in order to 
allow flights with a demand of 3 chutes to only use 2 chutes instead. In the following, this model 
is referred to as the Reduction Model, RM. The two steps are as follows: 
1. The Java algorithm introduced above in Table 10, is slightly modified so that for each 
flight with a demand of 3 chutes, objects using 2 chutes are also generated. To keep 
track of these reduced objects, a new matrix 𝑃𝑜 is generated. When an object o only 
using 2 chutes for a flight with a demand of 3 chutes is generated, 𝑃𝑜 is set to -1. The 
==PAGE:55==
45 
 
other matrices generated are unchanged. It has to be said that this step increases the 
complexity of the problem since it approximately doubles the amount of two-
dimensional objects generated for each flight with a demand of 3 chutes. 
2. RM, entirely based on MM2, is implemented in GAMS. One new set of binary variables 
𝑟𝑜 is added, and a new term is added in the objective function in order to ensure that 
reductions are only allowed in order to allocate flights that previously weren’t. These 
changes are displayed below. 
The objective function becomes: 
max          ∑∑∑𝑞𝑖 𝑐 𝑐′

. 𝑝 𝑐 𝑐′
𝑐 ∈ 𝐶
𝑐<𝑐′
𝑐′ ∈ 𝐶
 − 𝜆1 ∑𝑦𝑓
𝑓 ∈ 𝐹

𝑐 ∈ 𝐶
− 𝜆3 ∑∑𝑣𝑖 𝑐

𝑐 ∈ 𝐶
+ 𝜆4 ∑𝑃𝑜. 𝑥𝑜
𝑜 ∈ 𝑂
 
And the new set of constraints added is: 
𝑟𝑜= − 𝑃𝑜∗𝑥𝑜      ∀ 𝑜 ∈𝑂 
𝑟𝑜 ∈{0,1}              ∀ 𝑜 ∈𝑂 
The rest of RM is identical to MM2, see section 6.2.2. 
Regarding the value of the new penalty 𝜆4, it was chosen in order to ensure that objects using 
3 chutes would always be preferred unless the “reduction process” enables more flights to be 
allocated. Therefore, 𝜆4 had to be smaller than 𝜆1 but greater than 𝜆2 and 𝜆3, and more 
specifically, greater than a combination of 𝜆2 and 𝜆3. Given the values of the other penalties, 
see section 6.2.2, it was chosen to have 𝜆4 = 50. Note that the set variables 𝑟𝑜 is only added 
in order to be able to see for which flights (if any) 2 chutes are used instead of 3. 
For more details, all the codes, corresponding to MM2 and RM, implemented in both Java and 
GAMS can be found in Appendices B.2 and B.3. 
 
6.2.4 CONCLUSION ON MATHEMATICAL MODEL 2 
As explained before, MM2 was created in order to be able to control both the usage of the 
chutes by the three baggage handling companies and the sharing of the flexible chutes, while 
still minimizing the amount of flights left unallocated in the optimal solutions. Compared to 
MM1, this model is able to optimize the usage of the flexible chutes by limiting their sharing. 
Additionally, a different reward process was introduced with three main objectives. Firstly, as 
mentioned in section 6.2.2, the objective was to create a new reward process with a smaller 
complexity than the one introduced in MM1. Secondly, when a baggage handler needs to use 
the flexible chutes, the objective is still to reward more the usage of the closest flexible chutes 
==PAGE:56==
46 
 
to its own, and this is done by using two different types of reward in 𝑃 𝑐 𝑐′, see section 6.2.2. 
Lastly, 𝑃 𝑐 𝑐′ also rewards the usage of adjacent chutes which is a way to control that when the 
baggage handlers are not using all of their available chutes, the chutes that they actually use 
are grouped next to each other. 
Finally, there are two main drawbacks when using MM2. First of all, as mentioned in section 
6.2.2, MM2 has four main objectives which are combined together in the same objective 
function using penalties / coefficients. When combining different penalty terms – e.g. summing 
and subtracting terms – it becomes impossible to control each objective separately thus the 
values chosen for each penalty are critical. These values were first chosen arbitrarily and then 
adjusted in order to match all the different objectives of the model. Therefore, it has to be said 
that this process is time consuming, and it cannot be ensured that each of the different 
objectives is fulfilled to its optimality. Finally, another drawback of MM2 is its complexity. 
Indeed, while the amount of two-dimensional objects was not increased, four new sets of 
variables were added to the model. A comparison of the complexity can therefore be made 
between MM1 and MM2 by analysing the amount of variables and equations of each model, 
see Table 12. 
 
Table 12. Size of the two mathematical models (based on the data of Set 1 and on the original layout of BF3). 
 
Mathematical Model 1 
Mathematical Model 2 
Amount of variables 
𝑉1 = |𝑂| + |𝐹| 
10,214 ≤𝑉1 ≤18,512 
𝑉2 = |𝑂| + |𝐹| + 5|𝐶| + 3|𝐶|² 
46,402 ≤𝑉2 ≤54,700 
Amount of equations 
𝐸1 = |𝐹| + |𝐶|. |𝑇| + |𝑂| 
41,606 ≤𝐸1 ≤49,904 
𝐸2 = |𝐹| + |𝐶|. |𝑇| + 10|𝐶| + 6|𝐶|² 
103,973 ≤𝐸2 ≤104,132 
 
Looking at the numbers in Table 12, it is clear that the complexity of MM2 is drastically bigger 
than the one of MM1. More specifically, with MM2, in average, the amount of variables is 
multiplied by 3.7, and the amount of equations by 2.3 compared to MM1. At this point, it is 
difficult however to assess to what extent these changes will affect the computation times, and 
they will therefore be compared when solving the CAP in Chapter 7. To conclude, it can be 
said that the increase of the complexity in MM2 is a necessary compromise in order to be able 
to both access more information and control new parameters in BF3.

7. ANALYSIS OF THE RESULTS 
 
This chapter corresponds to the fourth and the sixth stages that were introduced in section 2.2. 
In this Chapter, using the two mathematical models introduced in Chapter 6, computations are 
performed in order to optimize the chutes usage in BF3, and therefore answer to the research 
question. The results are displayed in the following with a separation between MM1 (Stage 4), 
see section 7.1, and MM2 (Stage 6), see section 7.2. To be more specific, for both 
mathematical models, two different approaches are followed. The first approach consists in 
solving the CAP with the original layout of BF3, and, based on the results, incrementing small 
modifications by hand to the original layout of BF3. With this approach, the aim is to see to 
what extent these kind of modifications can improve the optimal solutions of the CAP. This 
approach is used both with MM1, in sections 7.1.1 and 7.1.2, and with MM2, in sections 7.2.1 
and 7.2.2. The second approach consists in considering all the chutes of BF3 flexible. This 
approach is used with MM1 in section 7.1.3 in order to see what would be the optimal solutions 
in BF3 if the baggage handlers were allowed to share all the chutes. Furthermore, this 
approach is also used with MM2 in section 7.2.3, and the aim is to let MM2 decide on the 
optimal split of the chutes between the three baggage handlers in each area of BF3, and on 
the optimal number of flexible chutes necessary to solve the CAP. All in all, these two 
approaches are devised in order to answer to the research question by creating new layouts 
with 8 flexible chutes more adapted for BF3, by determining the optimal number of flexible 
chutes in BF3, and by determining whether having less than 8 flexible chutes is a viable option 
for BF3. It can already be said that, following these two approaches, four layouts, more adapted 
to BF3, are created in this chapter. 
Additionally, as mentioned in Chapter 5, CPH Airport provided two datasets, and the focus of 
the project is on Set 1 only. Therefore, Chapter 7 only displays results based on Set 1. Only 
the most relevant results are displayed, and all the results found with Set 1 can actually be 
seen in Appendices D and E. Furthermore, the CAP was also solved with the data of Set 2, 
and the results are displayed in Appendix F.1. At this point, it has to be mentioned that Set 2 
was found to be less challenging for BF3 than Set 1. Therefore, the chutes demand of the 
flights of Set 2 were increased (the repartition of the chutes demand of the flights was kept 
similar to the one of Set 1) and Set 2-bis was created. For more information on the creation of 
Set 2-bis, see Appendix F.2. Using the data of Set 2-bis, the CAP was then solved for the 
original layout of BF3, and for the layouts mentioned above that were created based on Set 1. 
All the results based on Set 2-bis can also be seen in Appendix F.2. 
In the following section 7.1, the relevant results found with MM1 are displayed. 
==PAGE:58==
48

This section corresponds to the fourth stage that was introduced in section 2.2. More 
specifically, in section 7.1.1, the CAP is solved for the original layout of BF3 following two 
different cases. In the first case, all domestic flights are considered to have a demand of 1 
chute, and, in the second case, when possible, domestic flights are paired. Indeed, as 
mentioned in Chapter 2, domestic flights have a demand of 0.5 chute, meaning that the 
baggage handlers pair these flights when possible. In section 7.1.2, some modifications are 
applied to the original layout of BF3 and the results are analysed. Finally, a new approach is 
introduced in section 7.1.3 where all the chutes are considered flexible.

Case 2: Domestic flights paired (demand of 0.5 chute). 
As mentioned in Chapter 2, at CPH Airport, in theory domestic flights have a demand of 0.5 
chute. In other words, when possible, domestic flights are paired and the pairs are each 
allocated to the same chute. At CPH Airport, domestic flights are only paired when the two 
flights are handled by the same baggage handler, have the same loading type (container or 
belly wagons), and have STDs with a difference of maximum twenty minutes. Indeed, it is 
considered that pairing domestic flights that have STDs with a difference bigger than twenty 
minutes would monopolize the chutes for too long (for more than 31 TPs instead of 27 TPs 
when the flights are not paired). It can already be said that the total amount of flights that can 
be paired in each day at CPH Airport is rather small: around four pairs are made each day. 
For each day of Set 1, domestic flights are paired based on the above characteristics (same 
baggage handler, same loading type, and STDs with a difference of maximum twenty minutes). 
When a pair is created, a new ID is given to the resulting new flight, along with a new STD and 
a new opening time of the chute if necessary, and the chutes demand of the new flight is 1 
chute. At the end of the process, all the domestic flights that were not paired are left with a 
demand of 1 chute. In the following, this modified version of Set 1 is referred to as Set 1-bis. 
Furthermore, 3 pairs were made in Low, 6 pairs in Medium, 5 pairs in Busy, and 3 pairs in Very 
Busy, which confirms that the number of pairs that can be made in each day is rather small. 
Finally, in theory, domestic flights with a different loading type could also be paired, and these 
flights would need to be allocated to the blue chutes since it is the only type of chutes able to 
allocate both belly wagons and containers. However, the mathematical models introduced in 
Chapter 6 are unable to identify the types of the chutes, and some serious modifications would 
have to be done to the Java codes that generate the two-dimensional objects. Therefore, it is 
considered that the total amount of these possible pairs is too small to justify such a big 
increase in the complexity of the Java algorithms. 
MM1 is applied to Set 1-bis and to the original layout of BF3 introduced in section 2.1.1, and 
for each of the four days, the number of two-dimensional objects generated with Java, and the 
optimal solutions found with GAMS, can be seen in the following Table 16 and Table 17.

above results, the following section 7.1.2 aims at finding a new layout for BF3 through some 
modifications by hand to the original layout of BF3.

7.1.2 ANALYSIS WHEN CHANGES ARE MADE TO THE ORIGINAL LAYOUT OF BF3 
In this section, several modifications are made to the original layout of BF3, and, in total, five 
different layouts are tried. It can already be said that, in order to maximize the impact of the 
different changes, the changes are focused on the blue area. Indeed, the blue area is the only 
type of areas able to handle both containers and belly wagons. Furthermore, the changes 
displayed in this section mainly consist in giving chutes from one baggage handler to another 
one (mainly giving chutes from SGH to Aviator and Menzies). To do so, for logical reasons, 
adjacent chutes from the two baggage handlers are considered first. When there are no 
adjacent chutes between the two baggage handlers, the flexible chutes are reorganised in 
order to make the exchange of chutes possible. In the following, the five changes that are 
made to the original layout are introduced, and only the relevant results are presented and 
analysed. For more details on the changes that are made in this section, including the overview 
of the layouts that are tried and the associated optimal solutions, see Appendix D.2. Finally, 
all the computations are performed using Set 1-bis created in section 7.1.1, see Case 2, since 
the process of pairing the domestic flights is currently done at BF3 by the baggage handlers. 
1st change: 
The first change that is tried, based on the results presented in section 7.1.1, is to give 1 chute 
of SGH to Aviator (chute 45, see Appendix D.2). With this change, in total, 10 flights are 
unallocated in the optimal solutions (0 in Low, 1 flight handled by Menzies in Medium, 3 flights 
handled by SGH in Busy, and 6 flights handled by Aviator in Very Busy), and they correspond 
to a total demand of 18 chutes (3 chutes less compared to Case 2, see section 7.1.1). As for 
the flexible chutes usage, it is still unbalanced between the three baggage handling companies 
(51% for Aviator, 41% for Menzies and 8% for SGH). Therefore, compared to Case 2, giving 
one chute of SGH to Aviator did not create new un-allocations for SGH’s flights, and reduced 
by two flights the amount of un-allocations of Aviator’s flights. Additionally, the flexible chutes 
are still mainly used by Aviator and Menzies. The following changes therefore aim at seeing 
what happens when more chutes of SGH are given to Menzies and / or Aviator. 
2nd change: 
Based on the results of the 1st change, in this change, 1 chute of SGH is still given to Aviator 
(chute 45, same one as in the 1st change), and 1 chute of SGH is given to Menzies (chute 67, 
see Appendix D.2). With this change, in total, 9 flights are unallocated in the optimal solutions 
(0 in Low, 0 in Medium, 4 flights of SGH in Busy and 5 flights of Aviator in Very Busy), and they 
correspond to a total demand of 17 chutes (1 chutes less compared to the 1st change). It has 
to be noted that the un-allocations are now balanced between Aviator and SGH. Compared to 
the 1st change, it can be said that giving one extra chute to Menzies solved the un-allocation 
==PAGE:65==
55 
 
in Medium. As for Busy and Very Busy, there is no real improvement compared to the 1st 
change. To conclude, at this point, the un-allocations only occur for Aviator and SGH, therefore 
Menzies is not given any extra chutes in the following changes. 
3rd change: 
Based on the results of the 2nd change, in this change, 2 chutes of SGH are given to Aviator 
(chutes 44 and 45, see Appendix D.2), and 1 chute of SGH is given to Menzies (chute 67, 
same one as in the 2nd change). With this change, in total, 9 flights are unallocated in the 
optimal solutions (0 in Low, 0 in Medium, 4 flights of SGH in Busy and 5 flights of Aviator in 
Very Busy), and they correspond to a total demand of 16 chutes (1 chutes less, compared to 
the 2nd change). When looking into the un-allocations, they are still balanced between Aviator 
and SGH. However, compared to the 2nd change, there is no real improvement since the same 
amount of flights are unallocated. Finally, the flexible chutes usage is slightly more balanced 
between the three baggage handling companies compared to the previous changes (55% for 
Aviator, 31% for Menzies and 14% for SGH). Looking at the flexible chutes usage, it seems 
that, even if un-allocations occur for both Aviator and SGH, SGH can still give more of its own 
chutes to Aviator without creating more un-allocations in the optimal solutions. 
4th change: 
Based on the results of the 3rd change, in this change, 4 chutes of SGH are given to Aviator 
(chutes 43, 44, 45 and 100, see Appendix D.2), and 1 chute of SGH is given to Menzies (chute 
67, same one as in the 2nd and 3rd changes). The layout corresponding to this 4th change can 
be seen in Figure 13 in the following page, and the associated optimal solutions are displayed 
in the following Table 19 and Table 20.

un-allocations are still balanced between Aviator and SGH (4 for Aviator, only container flights,

un-allocations. As for the flexible chutes usage, it is finally balanced between the three 
baggage handlers compared to the previous changes (26% for Aviator, 42% for Menzies and

to allocate all of their flights without using the flexible chutes in Low. For more information, the 
Gantt charts of the flexible chutes for the remaining days (Medium, Busy and Very Busy) can

the 4th change, it is clearly the best solution so far in terms of un-allocations and flexible chutes 
usage. The un-allocations are balanced between Aviator and SGH meaning that giving more 
chutes of SGH to Aviator would reduce the un-allocations of Aviator in Busy whereas it would 
increase the ones of SGH in Very Busy.

5th change: 
Based on the results of the 4th change, no more changes regarding the amount of chutes given 
to each of the baggage handlers can be made without worsening the optimal solutions. 
Therefore, in this change, the modifications made in the 4th change are kept, see Figure 13, 
and a simple permutation of the chutes between the baggage handlers is added, see Appendix 
D.2. This permutation is made in order to be able to group the chutes of Menzies, since, in the 
original layout of BF3, chute 107 of Menzies is alone in the middle of Aviator’s chutes, see 
section 2.1.1. With this change, in total, 9 flights are unallocated in the final solution (0 in Low, 
0 in Medium, 4 flights from SGH in Busy, and 1 flight from SGH and 4 flights from Aviator in 
Very Busy), and they correspond to a total demand of 17 chutes, which is actually 1 chute 
greater than in the 4th case. It can therefore be concluded that this permutation is ineffective, 
and that the best layout so far is the one of the 4th change.

optimal solutions. Therefore, in the following section, a different approach is displayed in order 
to get some more information on the CAP using MM1.

7.1.3 ANALYSIS WHEN ALL THE CHUTES OF BF3 ARE CONSIDERED FLEXIBLE 
In this section, all the chutes in BF3 are considered flexible, meaning that they can be used, 
and shared, by any of the baggage handlers, and only the areas (red, blue and green) are 
kept. The aim is to see whether there still are some un-allocations in the optimal solutions 
when the 1st repartition constraints are dropped (According to the type of chutes, see section 
2.1.2). To be more specific, from the three sets of constraints introduced in section 2.1.2, the 
1st repartition constraints are the only ones that can be removed from the CAP. Indeed, the 2nd 
repartition (According to the type of area) is intrinsically linked to how BF3 was built since each 
area has its own working environment height in order to facilitate the use of containers and 
belly wagons. As for the adjacency principle, it is the core of the CAP. Therefore, dropping the 
1st repartition constraints is a way to visualize the un-allocations, if any, in the best case 
scenario (if all the baggage handlers were using the same equipment and were therefore able 
to share all the chutes of BF3). Looking for the optimal solutions in the best case scenario can 
be considered as the first key step when searching for an optimal layout for BF3. 
In agreement with CPH Airport, two different layouts are tried in this chapter. The first layout, 
F1, is the same as the original one of BF3, except that all the chutes are considered flexible. 
In the second layout, F2, the last minute chutes, introduced in section 2.1.2, are moved to the 
extremities of their row, and all the other chutes are also considered flexible. More specifically, 
the same number of last minute chutes is kept (10), along with the same repartition within the 
different areas (6 in the green area, 3 in the blue area, and 1 in the red area). Moving the last 
minute chutes should improve the optimal solutions since it increases the size of the 
workstations, and therefore reduces their amount (13 workstations in F1 and 8 workstations in 
F2). Compared to the layout F1, the layout F2 should facilitate the respect of the adjacency 
principle. For more information, the two layouts F1 and F2 are displayed in Appendix D.2. 
Finally, compared to MM1 that was introduced in section 6.1.2, in this chapter, some small 
adaptations are necessary. Indeed, since the baggage handlers are allowed to use and share 
all the chutes, there is no need for rewards anymore. Therefore, the only objective of the model 
is to minimize the amount of unallocated flights in the optimal solutions. However, even if the 
amount of constraints is decreased, the amount of variables is drastically increased since more 
two-dimensional objects are generated for each flight, see the following. Therefore, as it will 
be commented on, compared to the results of sections 7.1.1 and 7.1.2, the computational times 
are increased. Finally, the solver (GAMS) always considers the chutes in the same order, 
meaning that each flight is allocated to the first adjacent chutes available. In other words, this 
technique is a way to determine the minimum number of chutes necessary in each of the four 
days of Set 1-bis in order to solve the CAP. 
==PAGE:69==
59 
 
Layout F1: Based on the original layout of BF3. 
For Set 1-bis, using the layout F1, the number of two-dimensional objects generated with Java, 
using the algorithm introduced in section 6.1.1, is presented in the following Table 22, and the 
optimal solution found with GAMS, using the model introduced in section 6.1.2, including the 
adaptations mentioned above, can be seen in the following Table 23. 
Table 22. Computation times and amount of objects generated with Java for Set 1-bis with the layout F1.

is multiplied by 2.3, and the solving times by 7.4. Additionally, note that, since there are no 
rewards anymore, the objective values of Table 23 only display the number of unallocated 
flights in the optimal solutions (penalty of un-allocating a flight: 𝜆= 2). Therefore, compared to 
Case 2, only 1 flight is unallocated in Busy among the four days of Set 1-bis (12 flights in Case 
2, see section 7.1.1). 
Looking more specifically into each day, in Low, only 79 chutes out of the 109 available are 
used during the day. In Medium, only 97 chutes out of the 109 available are used during the 
day. In Busy, all the 109 chutes of BF3 are used during the day, and there is one flight that is 
unallocated: flight 44, handled by SGH with belly wagons and a demand of 3 chutes from TP 
52 to 90 (from 4:20 to 7:30). It can be said that the time of the un-allocation comprises the 
critical period of SGH that was identified in section 5.3.2 (from 6:10 to 6:35). Furthermore, 
when looking at the Gantt charts of this day, it can be seen that there are two chutes that are 
available from 4:20 to 7:30. In other words, if the demand of flight 44 was reduced from 3 
chutes to 2 chutes, this flight would be allocated as well. Finally, in Very Busy, all the 109 
chutes of BF3 are used during the day. It can be noted that the un-allocations that were 
foreseen in section 5.3.2 during this day do not exist anymore with this layout. In other words, 
it confirms that a more optimal layout should exist for BF3.

To conclude on F1, when all the chutes are considered flexible, only one un-allocation remains 
in Busy. Additionally, section 5.3.2 showed that, when only the 1st repartition constraints are 
kept (According to the type of chutes, see section 2.2), all the flights should be allocated in 
BF3, except for Very Busy where the maximum capacity of BF3 was exceeded. It is here 
verified that the adjacency principle and the 2nd repartition constraints (According to the type 
of areas, see section 2.2) also influence the CAP since all the flights are allocated in BF3 
except for Busy, see above. Therefore, it can be deduced that when including the 1st repartition 
constraints as well, it might be difficult to find a layout that would only leave one un-allocation 
in Busy, as it is the case here. In the following, the same analysis is performed using the layout 
F2 where the last minute chutes have been moved to the extremities of their row.

solving times are less important. In other words, it confirms that the position of the last minute 
chutes influences the optimal solutions. Not surprisingly, the longer the workstations are, the 
easier it is for the solver to respect the adjacency principle, and therefore find an optimal 
solution. Furthermore, the objectives values of Table 25 are identical to the ones found with

Regarding the amount of un-allocations and chutes used during each day, the results are the 
same as with the layout F1. Again, only one un-allocation occurs in Busy: flight 56, handled by 
SGH with belly wagons and a demand of 2 chutes from TP 68 to 94 (from 5:40 to 7:50). When 
looking at the Gantt charts of this day, there is also 1 chute available from 5:40 to 7:50, meaning 
that by reducing the demand of flight 56 from 2 chutes to 1 chute, this flight would be allocated 
as well. At this point, it can be highlighted that it is a flight with a demand of 2 chutes that is 
unallocated, compared to 3 chutes with the layout F1. In other words, it confirms that placing 
the last minute chutes at the extremities of their row is a better strategy for BF3. 
All in all, the same conclusions as for the layout F1 can be drawn. Even if including the 1st 
repartition constraints as well in the CAP is most likely to create other un-allocations, compared 
to the results of this section, it seems that a more optimal split of the chutes can be made 
between the three baggage handlers compared to the original layout of BF3. 
 
To conclude on section 7.1, and on MM1, several comments can be made. 
First of all, in section 7.1.1, the original layout of BF3 showed that there is a disproportion of 
usage of the flexible chutes between SGH and the other baggage handlers, and that flights 
are unallocated during 3 of the 4 days for all the baggage handlers. Additionally, it was proven 
that there are not enough domestic flights that can be paired in order to make an actual 
difference on the optimal solutions. It was agreed with CPH Airport that there is no point in 
pairing domestic flights with a difference of STDs greater than 20 minutes, and with different 
loading types or baggage handlers. 
Regarding the changes of section 7.1.2, they were able to reduce the total amount of 
unallocated flights and improve the flexible chutes usage. Additionally, they showed that each 
day is critical for one specific baggage handler only: Menzies in Medium, SGH in Busy, and 
Aviator in Very Busy. This increases the complexity of the CAP since it implies that none of the 
four days of Set 1-bis can be taken as a base in order to reach an optimal layout for BF3 but 
that the four days (mainly Medium, Busy, and Very Busy) have to be considered all at once 
when making changes to the original layout of BF3. 
Additionally, compared to what was pointed out in section 5.3, section 7.1.2 showed that SGH 
does not have a lot of spare chutes that can be given to the other baggage handlers since it 
experiences as many un-allocations as Aviator in most of the layouts that were tried. More 
specifically, looking at the loading types of the unallocated flights, SGH mainly experiences 
un-allocations for belly wagon flights, and Aviator for container flights. This tends to show that 
there might be a problem in the repartition of the chutes between the baggage handlers within 
the different areas (red, blue and green), as suspected in section 5.3. However, this point is 
==PAGE:72==
62 
 
difficult to assess when solving the CAP with MM1. Indeed, since no information are available 
regarding the usage of the chutes within the different areas, all the changes were only made 
in the blue area since it is the only area that is able to handle both loading types (containers 
and belly wagons). This aspect emphasizes the need for a second mathematical model that 
would be able to give more information on the usage of the chutes. 
Finally, section 7.1.2 showed that the more SGH gives chutes to Aviator, the more the use of 
the flexible chutes becomes balanced between the baggage handlers. This remark raises 
another important flaw of MM1. Indeed, the more balanced the usage of the flexible chutes 
becomes, the more important it is to actually optimize their usage in order to facilitate their 
management. Therefore, the need for a second mathematical model able to minimize the 
sharing of the flexible chutes also appear. 
Furthermore, in section 7.1.3, when considering all the chutes flexible, all the flights were 
allocated in each of the four days, except for one flight in Busy (for both layouts F1 and F2). 
These results highlighted the fact that a more optimal split of the chutes between the three 
baggage handlers should be possible in BF3. However, it has to be kept mind that, due to the 
complexity of the CAP, when including the 1st repartition constraints (According to the type of 
chutes) as well, it is most likely that there will be more than one un-allocation in the optimal 
solutions. Finally, once again, the need for a second mathematical model able to optimize the 
usage and the sharing of the chutes appears. Indeed, based the results of section 7.1.3, 
starting from the layout F2, an adequate mathematical model should be able to decide on the 
optimal repartition of the chutes between the three baggage handlers. This process is actually 
investigated in section 7.2.3 using MM2. 
Lastly, regarding the complexity of MM1, as mentioned in section 6.2.4, the computation times 
can be addressed. For all the layouts tried in section 7.1.1 and 7.1.2, the two-dimensional 
objects, created with Java, were all generated in less than 10 seconds, and the CAP was 
always solved in less than 5 minutes using GAMS. As for the two flexible layouts F1 and F2, 
the complexity was increased, and the two-dimensional objects were all generated in less than 
20 seconds, and the CAP always solved in less than 20 minutes. More specifically, with the 
layouts F1 and F2, in average, the generation times of the two-dimensional objects were 
increased by a factor 2.4, and the solving times of the CAP were increased by a factor 6.4. 
Given the size of the MM1, all these computation times are acceptable, and will be compared 
in section 7.2 with the ones obtained when using MM2. 
In the following section 7.2, all the results found with MM2 are presented and analysed. 
 
==PAGE:73==
63 
 
7.2 
ANALYSIS OF THE RESULTS WITH MATHEMATICAL MODEL 2 
This section corresponds to the sixth stage that was introduced in section 2.2. More 
specifically, in section 7.2.1, using MM2 and Set 1-bis, the CAP is solved again for the original 
layout of BF3 in order to see whether some new information regarding the chutes usage 
appear. From there, in section 7.2.2, new changes are applied to the original layout of BF3 
and the results are analysed. Finally, in section 7.2.3, a new approach is followed where MM2 
decides on the optimal split of the chutes between the three baggage handling companies. 
7.2.1 ANALYSIS OF THE CURRENT SITUATION IN BF3 
In this section, MM2 is applied to Set 1-bis using the original layout of BF3. For each of the 
four days of the set, the two-dimensional objects used are the same as the ones generated 
with Java in Case 2, see section 7.1.1. The optimal solutions found with GAMS, using the 
model introduced in section 6.2.2, can be seen in the following Table 26.

Note that, compared to MM1, since the objective function was changed, the objectives values

again, these numbers are only meant to be compared with other objective values found with 
MM2 when different layouts are tested. As for the solving times, in average the CAP was solved 
in 28 minutes, see Table 26, which is an increase of a factor 18 compared to MM1 (average 
solving time of 2.5 minutes), see Case 2 in section 7.1.1. Finally, the main information given 
by MM2 consists in knowing both whether there are any unallocated flights in the optimal 
solutions, and how the chutes are used by the baggage handlers in each day. 
Looking at the above Table 26, in total, 10 flights are unallocated in the final solution, and they 
correspond to a total demand of 24 chutes. Compared to Case 2, for each day, the amount of 
un-allocations is the same, except in Busy where 3 flights are unallocated instead of 5 flights. 
This is due to the fact that MM2 penalizes more the un-allocation of flights (𝜆1 = 500) than 
MM1 (𝜆= 2). Therefore, with MM2, it is mainly flights with a demand of 2 or 3 chutes that are 
unallocated in the optimal solutions. When looking into the corresponding amount of chutes, 
the results of the two models are actually equivalent. Furthermore, as expected, when looking 
into the unallocated flights with this model, the results are basically the same as in Case 2.

words, it confirms that Medium is critical for Menzies, Busy for SGH, and Very Busy for Aviator.

in Busy and Very Busy that are the most critical days (respectively 3 and 6 un-allocations). 
When looking more into the different areas, this is explained by the fact that Aviator is never 
using all of its 10 chutes in the green area, see the bold numbers in Table 28. As for the red 
and blue areas, they are entirely used by the baggage handlers during the four days. 
To conclude on section 7.2.1, the unused chutes from Aviator in the green area create un-
allocations that may be avoided, meaning that the repartition of the chutes between the 
different areas should be rethought. More specifically, Aviator needs fewer chutes in the green 
area and more chutes in the red and / or blue area. Indeed, Aviator is not using its own chutes 
from the green area but is using the flexible chutes (71% of the total usage) in order to allocate 
flights handled with containers. This confirms that a more optimal layout should exist for BF3. 
Therefore; in the following section 7.2.2, new changes are made to the original layout of BF3. 
 
7.2.2 ANALYSIS WHEN CHANGES ARE MADE TO THE ORIGINAL LAYOUT OF BF3 
Based on the results of section 7.2.1, more relevant changes are made to the original layout 
of BF3, and, in total, four new layouts are tried in this section. In order to keep track of the 
changes made in section 7.1.2, these new changes are counted starting from the 6th change. 
Compared to section 7.1.2 where changes had to be made in the blue area (only area able 
suitable for both containers and belly wagons) since no information were given on the usage 
of the chutes, the changes displayed in this section mainly consist in exchanging green chutes 
from Aviator with blue chutes from SGH. As in section 7.1.2, when chutes are exchanged 
between two baggage handlers, adjacent chutes from the two baggage handlers are 
considered first. When there are no adjacent chutes between the two baggage handlers, the 
flexible chutes are reorganised in order to make the exchange of chutes possible. In the 
following, the four changes that are made to the original layout are introduced, and only the 
relevant results are presented and analysed. For more details on the changes that are made 
in this section, including the overview of the layouts that are tried and the associated optimal 
solutions, see Appendix E.1. Again, all the computations are performed using Set 1-bis. 
6th change: 
In section 7.2.1, it was shown that, with the original layout of BF3, Aviator is never using all of 
its green chutes. Therefore, in this change, 2 green chutes of Aviator are exchanged with 2 
blue chutes of SGH (green chutes 47-48 given to SGH and blue chutes 99-100 given to Aviator, 
see Appendix E.1), and no change is made to the total number of chutes given to each 
baggage handler. With this exchange, in total, 8 flights are unallocated in the optimal solutions 
==PAGE:76==
66 
 
(0 in Low, 1 from Menzies in Medium, 2 from SGH in Busy and 5 from Aviator in Very Busy), 
and they correspond to a total demand of 20 chutes. Compared to the optimal solutions 
displayed in section 7.2.1, there is one un-allocation less in both Busy and Very Busy. As for 
the usage of the chutes in the different areas, it shows that Aviator is still not using all of its 
green chutes, see Appendix E.1. Therefore, green chutes of Aviator can still be exchanged 
with blue chutes of SGH. Finally, regarding the flexible chutes usage, the same disproportion 
of usage from Aviator is witnessed. More specifically, including the shared chutes, Aviator is 
using 72% of the flexible chutes, Menzies 48% and SGH 28%. In other words, it suggests that 
some of the chutes of SGH can be given to Aviator. To conclude, compared to the optimal 
solutions displayed in section 7.2.1, the exchange of 2 blue chutes from SGH with 2 green 
chutes from Aviator enabled to reduce the un-allocations in both Busy and Very Busy. 
7th change: 
In this change, the changes introduced in the 6th change are kept, and 3 blue chutes of SGH 
are exchanged with 1 green chute of Menzies and 2 green chutes of Aviator (green chutes 49-
51 given to SGH, blue chute 67 given to Menzies and blue chutes 96-97 given to Aviator, see 
Appendix E.1). With this exchange, in total, 5 flights are unallocated in the optimal solutions (0 
in Low, 1 from Menzies in Medium, 1 from SGH in Busy and 3 from Aviator in Very Busy), and 
they correspond to a total demand of 13 chutes. Compared to the 6th change, there are one 
un-allocation less in Busy and two in Very Busy. Additionally, in this change, all the 109 chutes 
are used in both Busy and Very Busy, which shows that some simple exchanges of chutes 
between the baggage handlers are enough to improve the chutes usage. Regarding the 
flexible chutes usage, including the shared chutes, Aviator is using 59% of the flexible chutes, 
Menzies 68% and SGH 45%, see Appendix E.1. It can also be said that no flexible chutes are 
used in Low. Finally, there are two unused flexible chutes in Medium, and two flexible chutes 
are fully given to Aviator in both Busy and Very Busy, see Appendix E.1. All in all, it suggests 
that a layout where Aviator is given 2 chutes from SGH should be tried. 
8th change: 
In this change, the modifications of the 7th change are kept, and 2 blue chutes of SGH are 
given to Aviator (chutes 78-79, see Appendix E.1). With these changes, in total, 4 flights are 
unallocated in the optimal solutions (0 in Low, 1 from Menzies in Medium, 1 from SGH in Busy 
and 2 from Aviator in Very Busy), and they correspond to a total demand of 11 chutes. 
Compared to the 7th change, there is one un-allocation less in Very Busy, and all the 109 
chutes are used in both Busy and Very Busy. Finally, the flexible chutes usage is balanced 
between the baggage handlers (28% for Aviator, 46% for Menzies and 26% for SGH), and 
there are, in total, 13 shared chutes in the optimal solutions (5 flexible chutes shared by all 3 
baggage handlers, and 8 flexible chutes shared by 2 out of the 3 baggage handlers, see 
==PAGE:77==
67 
 
Appendix E.1). To conclude, giving 2 blue chutes from SGH to Aviator did not worsen the 
optimal solution of Busy (critical day for SGH) and improved the one of Very Busy (critical day 
for Aviator). Furthermore, looking at the chutes usage, including the flexible chutes, it seems 
that no more changes can be made in the repartition of the chutes between the baggage 
handlers without worsening the optimal solutions, see Appendix E.1. Additionally, the un-
allocations are balanced between Aviator and SGH, and giving more chutes of SGH to Aviator 
would remove the un-allocation of Aviator in Busy whereas it would create more un-allocations 
for SGH in Very Busy. In the following, a last layout is tried where more green chutes of Aviator 
are exchanged with blue chutes of SGH, in order to determine whether this type of exchange 
can still improve the optimal solutions. 
9th change: 
In this change, the changes introduced in the 8th change are kept, and 4 green chutes of Aviator 
are exchanged with 4 blue chutes of SGH (green chutes 106-109 given to SGH and blue 
chutes 77 and 93-95 given to Aviator, see Appendix E.1). In the following, the layout 
corresponding to the 9th change, and the associated optimal solutions, are displayed in Figure 
14, and Table 29 and Table 30. 
Figure 14. Layout of the 9th change. Yellow: Aviator, 20 chutes. Blue: Menzies, 10 chutes. Green: SGH, 71 chutes. 
Red: flexible chutes, 8 chutes. (Red font: green chutes of Aviator and Menzies exchanged with blue chutes of SGH, 
and blue chutes 78-79 of SGH given to Aviator).

Note that, compared to the original layout of BF3, see section 7.2.1, in average, the objective 
values are reduced by 32% and the solving times by a factor 1.8 (average solving time of 15 
minutes), see Table 30, which confirms that this layout is more optimal for BF3. Additionally, 
there is the same repartition of unallocated flights as in the 8th change (0 in Low, 1 from 
Menzies in Medium, 1 from SGH in Busy and 2 from Aviator in Very Busy), and they also 
correspond to a total demand of 11 chutes. As for the flexible chutes usage, it is also 
comparable to the one of the 8th change, and there are, in total, 14 shared chutes in the optimal 
solutions (2 shared by all the baggage handlers, and 12 shared by 2 out of 3 of the baggage 
handlers, see Appendix E.1). The only difference with the 8th change lies in the fact that, in this 
change, there are fewer chutes that are shared by all three baggage handlers. For this reason, 
it is considered that the 9th change provides better optimal solutions than the 8th change. 
To conclude, the 9th change is the best layout that can be achieved by directly modifying the 
original layout of BF3. Indeed, it was shown in the 8th change that no more changes can be 
made in the repartition of the chutes between the baggage handlers without worsening the 
optimal solutions. Compared to the original layout of BF3, the un-allocations are reduced by 
40% (from 10 to 4 flights unallocated), and the flexible chutes usage is increased by 21%. This 
increase is due to the fact that, in total, more flights are allocated in the optimal solutions, 
compared to Case 2. Therefore, on overall, the chutes in BF3 are being used more extensively. 
 
To conclude on section 7.2.2, the following Table 31 and Table 32 give an overview of the 
improvement that were achieved in this section. More specifically, based on the results found 
with the original layout of BF3, see section 7.2.1, and the results of the 9th change, Table 31 
displays the amount of unused chutes and unallocated flights in the optimal solutions and Table 
32 details how the flexible chutes are shared between the baggage handlers.

Based on Table 31, compared to Case 2, it is verified that in the 9th change the total amount 
of unallocated flights is reduced by 60% (from 10 to 4 unallocated flights) and, when excluding 
Low, the total amount of unused chutes is reduced by 38% (from 8 to 5 unused chutes). 
Note that Table 32 takes into account the shared chutes when calculating how many flexible 
chutes each baggage handler uses in each day, which explains why the sums of the 
percentages are greater than 100% in the higher part of the table. Furthermore, when looking 
at Table 32, compared to Case 2, in the 9th change the total amount of flexible chutes used 
during the four days is reduced by 27% (from 7.5 to 5.5 flexible chutes used in average), which 
confirms that the layout of the 9th change is more suitable for BF3 compared to the original 
one. Additionally, it can be seen that the total amount of shared chutes (shared by 2 or 3 of the 
baggage handlers) is increased by 25% (from 3 to 4 shared chutes) in the 9th change. In other 
words, even if the total usage of the flexible chutes is reduced, the total amount of shared 
chutes is increased in the 9th change. This can be explained by the fact that, in the 9th change, 
the usage of the flexible chutes is more balanced between the baggage handlers compared to 
Case 2, therefore more flexible chutes are shared during the 4 days. 
==PAGE:80==
70 
 
All in all, a new layout able to give better results than the original one of BF3 was determined 
in this section (9th change). Indeed, compared to Case 2, the un-allocations are reduced, the 
flexible chutes usage is improved, and there are no more unused chutes from Aviator in both 
Busy and Very Busy. Additionally, compared to the layout of the 4th change, determined in 
section 7.1.2, the 9th change also provides better results. Indeed, in the 4th change, in total, 9 
flights were unallocated in the optimal solutions, compared to 4 flights in the 9th change. This 
confirms that MM2 is able to determine more relevant information on the chutes usage 
compared to MM1. However, there is no way to determine whether the layout of the 9th change 
is an optimal one for BF3. More specifically, even if the usage of the chutes is improved in the 
9th change, it cannot be determined whether the repartition of the chutes between the baggage 
handlers in each area is optimal. Therefore, the need for a new approach using MM2 appears. 
Indeed, it should be MM2 that decides on the optimal split of the chutes in each area between 
the three baggage handlers, and that identifies the minimum number of flexible chutes 
necessary in order to solve the CAP. This approach is displayed in the following section 7.2.3. 
 
7.2.3 ANALYSIS WHEN MM2 DECIDES ON THE OPTIMAL SPLIT OF THE CHUTES IN BF3 
Based on the limits of section 7.2.2, in this section, a new approach is displayed. More 
specifically, all the chutes are considered flexible, as in section 7.1.3, and, it is MM2 that aims 
at splitting all the chutes between the three baggage handlers. Indeed, MM2 is able both to 
minimize the sharing of the chutes and to group the chutes given to each baggage handler. 
Therefore, this section has three main objectives. First of all, with this method, the minimum 
number of flexible chutes necessary in BF3 in order to solve the CAP can be determined. 
Moreover, this method is also able to devise an optimal layout for BF3 with 8 flexible chutes 
that can be compared to the original layout of BF3. Finally, this method is a way to establish 
whether a layout with less than 8 flexible chutes is a viable option for BF3. 
Furthermore, given the results of section 7.2.2, and in agreement with CPH Airport, only the 
layout F2 is considered in the following. Indeed, it is considered that, in an optimal layout, last 
minute chutes should be located at the end of the rows, and not in the middle of them, as it is 
currently the case in BF3. Additionally, in this section, the CAP is again solved using Set 1-bis. 
It can already be said that, given the size of the CAP when all the chutes are considered 
flexible, see section 7.1.3, and the increase in the complexity when using MM2 compared to 
MM1, see section 6.2.4, only feasible solutions, and not optimal ones, can be found. Therefore, 
in order to find the best layout possible for BF3, a logical strategy with 4 steps is devised, see 
the following. 
==PAGE:81==
71 
 
Step 1: All the chutes considered flexible (layout F2). 
The following Table 33 and Table 34 display the solutions found using MM2 on the layout F2 
and Set 1-bis. It is verified that the amount of objects generated in each day, see Table 33, is 
identical to the ones of section 7.1.3 when all the chutes were considered flexible and MM1 
was applied, see Table 24. However, compared to Table 24, the execution times are increased 
since more outputs are generated in Java for MM2 compared to MM1, see section 6.2.1. 
Table 33: Computation times and amount of objects generated with Java for Set 1-bis in the layout F2.

First of all, looking at Table 34, it can be seen that, for each day, the solver run with a time limit 
of 100,000 seconds, which corresponds to approximately 28 hours, and an optimal solution 
was only found in Low in around 16 hours. These numbers confirm that the complexity of the 
CAP becomes too important when all the chutes are considered flexible and MM2 is applied. 
In other words, based on the above results, it is not possible to directly determine how many 
chutes should be let flexible in BF3 and how many chutes should be given to each of the 
baggage handlers in each of the three areas of BF3. It has to be said that a time limit of 100,000 
seconds is highly critical, and this is discussed in Chapter 8. In the following, the results of 
each day are briefly analysed. For more details on the solutions, see Appendix E.2. 
 
In Low, 85 chutes out of 109 used, which is 6 chutes more than in section 7.1.3 where the 
chutes could all be shared by the baggage handlers. Furthermore, all the flights are allocated, 
and no chutes are shared between the baggage handlers. For more information; Figure 15 in 
the following page displays the layout of this optimal solution.

Based on Figure 15, it can be seen that Aviator is given 17 chutes (14 blue and 3 green chutes), 
Menzies 8 chutes (all blue chutes), and SGH 60 chutes (5 red, 39 blue and 16 green chutes). 
It is verified that this layout is optimal since the chutes given to each baggage handler are all 
grouped, and there are no shared chutes. However, due to the fact that there are not enough 
flights in this day to make it a challenging one, the red area is mainly unused. Therefore, this 
solution, even if it is an optimal one, is not representative of how each area should be split 
between the three baggage handlers or of how many chutes should be let flexible in BF3. 
 
In Medium, a feasible but not optimal solution is found where 99 chutes out of 109 are used. 
More specifically, Aviator is given 7 chutes (all blue chutes), Menzies 12 chutes (5 blue and 7 
green chutes), SGH 62 chutes (15 red, 33 blue and 14 green chutes), and there are 18 shared 
chutes (11 between Aviator and SGH, and 7 between Aviator and Menzies). Additionally, four 
flights are un-allocated in this feasible solution, three handled by Menzies and one by Aviator: 
- 
100: handled by Menzies with belly wagons, demand of 1 chute from TP 94 to 120. 
- 
194: handled by Menzies with containers, demand of 3 chutes from TP 141 to 179. 
- 
283: handled by Menzies with belly wagons, demand of 2 chutes from TP 201 to 227. 
- 
298: handled by Aviator with containers, demand of 2 chutes from TP 208 to 234. 
Figure 15: Optimal solution found in Low using MM2 on Set 1-bis with the layout F2 (Yellow: Aviator, 17 chutes. 
Blue: Menzies, 8 chutes. Green: SGH, 60 chutes. White: unused chutes, 24 chutes). 
==PAGE:83==
73 
 
It can be said that compared to the Linear Programing (LP) relaxation, there is a gap of 91% 
with the objective value that was found, see Table 34, which shows that the feasible solution 
found is far from an optimal one (this can also be seen from the number of shared chutes and 
un-allocations). For more information, the layout of this solution is displayed in Appendix E.2. 
Looking at the layout, it can be seen that the above un-allocations are easily avoidable. Indeed, 
3 blue chutes can be given to Menzies (chutes 52 to 54 for instance) in order to allocate flights 
100, 194 and 283, since their opening times do not overlap, and 2 blue chutes can be given to 
Aviator (chutes 78 and 79 for instance) in order to allocate flight 298. With these modifications, 
Medium would be solved with 104 chutes (9 to Aviator, 15 to Menzies, 62 to SGH and 18 
shared chutes) and no un-allocations. However, even with these modifications, given the 
amount of shared chutes and the fact that when all the chutes could be shared Medium was 
solved with 97 chutes only, see section 7.1.3, this layout remains far from optimal. 
 
In Busy, a feasible but not optimal solution is found where all 109 chutes are used. More 
specifically, Aviator is given 21 chutes (16 blue and 5 green chutes), Menzies 8 chutes (all blue 
chutes), SGH 71 chutes (19 red, 40 blue and 12 green chutes), and 9 chutes are shared (6 
between Menzies and SGH, 1 between Aviator and Menzies, 1 between Aviator and SGH, and 
1 by all three baggage handlers). Additionally, as in section 7.1.3 where all the chutes could 
be shared, there is one flight unallocated in the solution: flight 44, handled by SGH with belly 
wagons and a demand of 3 chutes from TP 52 to 90 (from 4:20 to 7:30). It can be mentioned 
that this un-allocation occurs at the same period of time as the one of section 7.1.3 when using 
the layout F2 (flight 56, handled by SGH with belly wagons and a demand of 2 chutes from 
5:40 to 7:50). Therefore, also taking into account that compared to the LP relaxation there is a 
gap of only 13% with the objective value that was found, see Table 34, this solution is not too 
far from an optimal one. To be more specific, in a more optimal layout for BF3, there would be 
less than 9 shared chutes (as it the case here). For instance, in section 7.2.2, in the 9th change, 
Busy was solved with only one flight unallocated (flight 44, same as here), and only 8 shared 
chutes. For more information; the following Figure 16 displays the layout of this feasible 
solution. 
==PAGE:84==
74 
 
 
Finally, in Very Busy, a feasible but not optimal solution is found where all 109 chutes are used. 
More specifically, Aviator is given 27 chutes (9 red, 14 blue and 4 green chutes), Menzies 7 
chutes (all blue), SGH 61 chutes (6 red, 39 blue and 16 green chutes), and 14 chutes are 
shared (7 between Menzies and SGH, 1 between Aviator and Menzies, and 6 between Aviator 
and SGH). Additionally, five flights are un-allocated in the solution, all handled by SGH: 
- 
19: handled by SGH with containers, demand of 1 chute from TP 52 to 78. 
- 
32: handled by SGH with containers, demand of 2 chutes from TP 55 to 81. 
- 
79: handled by SGH with containers, demand of 1 chute from TP 79 to 105. 
- 
87: handled by SGH with belly wagons, demand of 2 chutes from TP 83 to 113. 
- 
158: handled by SGH with belly wagons, demand of 1 chute from TP 139 to 165. 
It can be said that compared to the Linear Programing (LP) relaxation, there is a gap of 99% 
with the objective value that was found, see Table 34, which shows that the feasible solution 
found is far from an optimal one. To be more specific, in a more optimal layout for BF3, there 
would be less shared chutes and unallocated flights. For instance, in section 7.2.2, in the 9th 
change, Very Busy was solved with only 2 un-allocations and 8 shared chutes. For more 
information, the layout of this solution is displayed in Appendix E.2.

To conclude on the results found in Step 1, the complexity of the CAP becomes too challenging 
when all the chutes are considered flexible and MM2 is applied. More specifically, only feasible 
solutions, usually far from optimal ones, are found, and are not representative of how many 
flexible chutes should remain in BF3 and how the different areas should be split between the 
three baggage handlers. Furthermore, as it was mentioned as the end of section 7.1, each day 
is critical for one of the baggage handlers only (Medium for Menzies, Busy for SGH and Very 
Busy for Aviator) meaning that no day can be chosen as a base in order to find an optimal 
layout for BF3. Therefore, in the following Step 2, the results of the four days found in this step 
are considered all at once in order to devise two different strategies that can be applied to BF3. 
 
Step 2: Two possible strategies for BF3. 
It was agreed with CPH Airport that the results of Step 1 should be used as a base in order to 
devise an optimal layout for BF3. It was decided to start with 15 flexible chutes, all located in 
the blue area, and to split the remaining 94 chutes (19 red, 22 green and 53 blue chutes) 
between the baggage handlers according to the results of Step 1. To summarize the results of 
Step 1, Table 35 recaps the repartition of the chutes in each area, and Table 36 shows how 
the shared chutes are balanced between the three baggage handlers in each day.

Excluding Low, there is an average of 14 shared chutes per day, see Table 36. Therefore, 
starting with 15 flexible chutes, as it was agreed on with CPH Airport, is a logical first milestone. 
==PAGE:86==
76 
 
Furthermore, when looking at the repartition of the chutes in the red area in Table 35, it can be 
seen that Menzies is never given any red chutes and that Aviator is only given red chutes in 
Very Busy. Therefore, two different strategies emerge: 
1. 
The whole red area can be given to SGH, as it is currently the case in BF3. 
2. 
The red area can be shared between Aviator and SGH. Excluding Low that is not a 
representative day, in average SGH is given 14 red chutes out of the 19 available, see 
Table 35. Therefore, a possible repartition is to give 14 red chutes to SGH and 5 red 
chutes to Aviator. Additionally, these numbers are consistent with the ones determined 
during the data analysis in Chapter 5. More specifically, section 5.3 showed that in 
average 50% of the flights of both SGH and Aviator are handled with containers, and 
that SGH handles 70% of the flights in BF3 while Aviator handles 20%. Taking all this 
into account, it can be said that 75% of the red area can be given to SGH and 25% to 
Aviator, which also leads to 14 red chutes to SGH and 5 red chutes to Aviator. 
Regarding the repartition of the chutes in the green area, based on Table 35, in average, 15 
green chutes are given to SGH, instead of 6 green chutes as it is currently the case in BF3. In 
other words, it clearly shows that SGH should be given more chutes from the green area 
compared to what is currently done in BF3. As for Menzies, it is only given green chutes in 
Medium, and when looking at the average of the four days, Menzies is given 2 green chutes, 
see Table 35. This shows that in general Menzies is only given blue chutes due to the small 
amount of chutes that it needs in total in each day (9 chutes in average, see Table 35). Given 
that around 90% of Menzies’ flights are belly wagon flights, Menzies should still be given chutes 
from the green area. Finally, regarding Aviator, it is allocated an average of 3 green chutes (4 
when excluding Medium) during the four days, see Table 35. Therefore, a possible split of the 
green area is to give 16 green chutes to SGH, 4 green chutes to Aviator, and 2 green chutes 
to Menzies. Furthermore, these numbers are also consistent with the flights repartition found 
in section 5.3: 70% of the flights handled by SGH, 20% by Aviator, and 10% by Menzies. 
At this point, considering the above two strategies, the baggage handlers are already given: 
Strategy 1 (red area to SGH): 
 
SGH: 19 red + 16 green = 35 chutes 
 
Aviator: 4 green chutes 
 
Menzies: 2 green chutes 
TOTAL: 40 chutes 
Strategy 2 (red area to Aviator and SGH): 
 
SGH: 14 red + 16 green = 30 chutes 
 
Aviator: 5 red + 4 green = 9 chutes 
 
Menzies: 2 green chutes 
TOTAL: 40 chutes 
Only the blue area remains to be split between the baggage handlers. Considering what was 
stated above, as a start, 15 blue chutes are considered flexible, leaving 53 blue chutes to split 
between the baggage handlers. Looking at Menzies, in average during the four days, it is given 
9 chutes, see Table 35. Considering that 2 green chutes are already given to Menzies in the 
==PAGE:87==
77 
 
two strategies, it should be given at least 7 blue chutes. Furthermore, based on Table 35, an 
average of 18 chutes is given to Aviator during the four days, meaning that it should be given 
at least 14 blue chutes in Strategy 1 and 9 blue chutes in Strategy 2. As for SGH, based on 
Table 35, it is given an average of 63 chutes during the four days, meaning that it should be 
given at least 28 blue chutes in Strategy 1 and 33 blue chutes in Strategy 2. With these two 
new repartitions, a total of 49 blue chutes is attributed to the baggage handlers in each 
strategy. In order to start with 15 flexible chutes only, 4 more blue chutes have to be split 
between the baggage handlers in both strategies. To do so, the flights repartition displayed in 
section 5.3 is used. Considering that 70% of the flights are handled by SGH, 20% by Aviator, 
and 10% by Menzies, it is chosen to give 3 more blue chutes to SGH and 1 more blue chute 
to Aviator. To conclude, the two possible strategies with 15 flexible chutes are as follows: 
Strategy 1 (red area to SGH): 
 
SGH: 19 red + 16 green +31 blue = 66 chutes 
 
Aviator: 4 green +15 blue = 19 chutes 
 
Menzies: 2 green + 7 blue = 9 chutes 
 
Flexible: 15 blue chutes 
TOTAL: 109 chutes 
Strategy 2 (red area to Aviator and SGH): 
 
SGH: 14 red + 16 green + 36 blue = 66 chutes 
 
Aviator: 5 red + 4 green + 10 blue = 19 chutes 
 
Menzies: 2 green + 7 blue = 9 chutes 
 
Flexible: 15 blue chutes 
TOTAL: 109 chutes 
 
Note that the amount of chutes given to each baggage handler in the two strategies is identical, 
and it is only the repartition of the chutes within each area that is different. The two layouts 
with 15 flexible chutes, based on the above strategies, can be seen in Appendix E.2. In the 
following, these two layouts are denoted S1_15 for Strategy 1, and S2_15 for Strategy 2. 
 
To conclude on Step 2, several comments can be made. First of all, a final layout for BF3 
cannot remain with 15 flexible chutes, therefore, the above two layouts are used in the following 
Step 3 in order to find a layout that would suit all of the four days with a minimum number of 
flexible chutes. Additionally, the reliability of the method displayed in Step 2 can be questioned. 
Indeed, excluding Low that is not a representative day, the solutions found in Step 1 were not 
optimal. In other words, it cannot be argued that the two above layouts are optimal ones for 
BF3. As for the split of each area and the location of the flexible chutes, it can also be argued 
whether the areas should have been split as they were in Step 2, and whether the flexible 
chutes should all be located in the blue area. It can be said that these choices were made 
following a logical development based on both the results of Step 1 and the data analysis 
devised in section 5.3. All in all, there is no guaranty that these two strategies are optimal ones, 
however, given the method that was chosen, they seem to be close to optimal ones. 
==PAGE:88==
78 
 
Step 3: Results when applying MM2 to the two strategies. 
Using the two strategies of Step 2, there are three main objectives in Step 3. The first objective 
is to devise an optimal layout for BF3 with a minimum number of flexible chutes. Secondly, 
another objective is to determine an optimal layout with 8 flexible chutes in order to be able to 
compare it to the original layout of BF3 and to the 9th change (best layout with 8 flexible chutes 
created so far, see section 7.2.2). Finally, the last objective is to create a layout with less than 
8 flexible chutes in order to see to what extent it influences the optimal solutions. 
It can already be said that the two strategies of Step 2 provide similar results in terms of un-
allocations and optimal values. The optimal values obtained with Strategy 1 being slightly 
better than the ones of Strategy 2, only the relevant results of Strategy 1 are displayed in the 
following. For more details on the two strategies, including the overview of the layouts that are 
created in both cases and the associated optimal solutions, see Appendix E.2. 
Results with the layout S1_15: 
As a start, the results obtained with the layout S1_15, using the MM2 on Set 1-bis, are 
displayed in the following Table 37 and Table 38.

Note that, compared to Step 1, where all the chutes could be shared, the average amount of

solutions were found in each day with important computation times, see Table 38, since in 
average, the solver run 52 minutes before finding an optimal solution. However, in the 9th 
change, see section 7.2.2, in average, the solver run 15 minutes before finding an optimal 
solution. In other words, when going from 8 flexible chutes (9th change) to 15 flexible chute 
(S1_15), the computation times are increased by a factor of 4.6. Therefore, it highlights the 
need to reduce the amount of flexible chutes in BF3. Finally, note that, in total, there is only

one un-allocation in Busy, which is the smallest possible amount of un-allocations in BF3 with 
Set 1-bis, as detailed in section 7.1.3. In the following, the above optimal solutions are briefly 
analysed, and the complete optimal results of each day can be seen in Appendix E.2. 
In Low, 85 chutes out of the 109 available are used. More specifically, Aviator is given 17 
chutes, Menzies 8 chutes and SGH 60 chutes. All the flights are allocated, there are no shared 
chutes, and no flexible chutes are used either. It is actually the same repartition of the chutes 
as the one of the optimal solution found in Step 1 when all the chutes were considered flexible. 
In Medium, 107 chutes out of the 109 available are used. More specifically, Aviator is given 17 
chutes, Menzies 19 chutes and SGH 71 chutes. All the flights are allocated, and two chutes 
from Aviator are unused. There are no shared chutes, however all the flexible chutes are used 
and given to one of the baggage handlers (5 to SGH and 10 to Menzies). This already shows 
that reducing the amount of flexible chutes might create un-allocations in this day. 
In Busy, all the 109 chutes are used. More specifically, Aviator is given 21 chutes, Menzies 9 
chutes, SGH 71 chutes, and 8 chutes are shared (2 between Aviator and SGH, 5 between 
Menzies and SGH, and 1 by all three baggage handlers). In other words, all the flexible chutes 
are used, and 7 out of 15 are used only by of the baggage handlers (5 by SGH and 2 by 
Aviator). There is one flight that is un-allocated in the final solution: flight 44, handled by SGH 
with belly wagons and a demand of 3 chutes from TP 52 to 90. It is the same un-allocation as 
in Step 1 when all the chutes were considered flexible, and it can be said that it is actually the 
best that can be achieved in this day, in terms of un-allocations, as it was seen in section 7.1.3. 
In very Busy, all the 109 chutes of BF3 are used. More specifically, Aviator is given 20 chutes, 
Menzies 11 chutes, SGH 66 chutes, and 12 chutes are shared (11 between Aviator and SGH, 
and 1 between Aviator and Menzies). In other words, all the flexible chutes are used, and 3 
out of 15 are used only by one baggage handler (2 by Menzies and 1 by Aviator). All the flights 
are allocated, and no chutes are shared by all the baggage handlers. However, 11 flexible 
chutes are shared between Aviator and SGH which suggests that they both need extra chutes.

of the chutes between the three baggage handlers and the flexible chutes during the four days.

Looking at Table 40, none of the baggage handlers is given one (or more) of the flexible chutes 
for all the 4 days. This is due to the fact that, in both Busy and Very Busy, most of the flexible 
chutes are shared by the baggage handlers (respectively 8 and 12 shared chutes). Therefore, 
reducing the total number of flexible chutes, is most likely to create new un-allocations. In other 
words, 15 flexible chutes seem to be the minimum number of flexible chutes in BF3 in order to 
avoid un-allocations. However, as mentioned at the end of Step 2, a final layout for BF3 cannot 
remain with 15 flexible chutes. 
In order to reduce the total amount of flexible chutes, some logical decisions have to be made. 
Firstly, SGH is given 5 flexible chutes in both Medium and Busy, and is sharing 11 chutes with 
Aviator in Very Busy. Secondly, Aviator is only given flexible chutes in Busy and Very Busy, 
and Menzies in Medium and Very Busy. Based on these observations, a new layout with 11 
flexible chutes is tried, S1_11, where SGH is given 2 extra blue chutes, and both Aviator and 
Menzies 1 extra blue chute. 
 
Results with the layout S_11: 
The layout S1_11 and the associated optimal results in the four days can be seen in Appendix 
E.2. It will only be mentioned here that, compared to S1_15, there is one extra un-allocation in 
Very Busy. Based on the results of the layout S1_11, see Appendix E.2, a new layout with 9 
flexible chutes is tried, S1_9, where SGH is given 2 extra blue chutes. 
 
Results with the layout S1_9:
