In Low, 85 chutes out of the 109 available are used. More specifically, Aviator is given 17 
chutes, Menzies 8 chutes and SGH 60 chutes. All the flights are allocated, there are no shared 
chutes, and 1 flexible chute is used and given to Aviator. It is basically the same solution as in 
S1_15 and S1_11. This is also confirmed by the fact that the objective value found with S1_9 
in Low is similar to the one found with S1_15, see Table 42. 
In Medium, 106 chutes out of the 109 available are used. More specifically, Aviator is given 17 
chutes, Menzies 18 chutes, SGH 70 chutes, and 1 chute is shared between Menzies and SGH. 
All the flights are allocated, and three chutes from Aviator are unused. In other words, all the 
flexible chutes are used and 8 out of 9 are used only by Menzies. At this point, it can be seen 
that Menzies is using all of the flexible chutes, meaning that giving an extra chute (or more) to 
Aviator or SGH is most likely to create at least one un-allocation in that day. Finally, note that 
the objective value found with S1_9 in Medium is also similar to the one found with S1_15, see 
Table 42, which confirms that the two solutions are almost identical. 
In Busy, all the 109 chutes of BF3 are used. More specifically, Aviator is given 20 chutes, 
Menzies 10 chutes, SGH 70 chutes, and all the flexible chutes are shared (4 between Aviator 
and SGH, and 5 between Menzies and SGH). There is one flight that is un-allocated in the final 
solution: flight 44, handled by SGH with belly wagons and a demand of 3 chutes from TP 52 
to 90. At this point it can be said that, compared to S1_15 and S1_11, the solution was not 
worsen, even if the number of flexible chutes was reduced, since there is still only one un-
allocation in this day. This is also confirmed by the fact that the objective value found with S1_9 
in Busy is similar to the one found with S1_15, see Table 42. However, all the flexible chutes 
are now shared, and SGH is using all of them. In other words, giving an extra chute (or more) 
to Aviator or Menzies is most likely to create at least one un-allocation in that day. 
Finally, in Very Busy, all the 109 chutes of BF3 are used. More specifically, Aviator is given 20 
chutes, Menzies 10 chutes, SGH 70 chutes, and all the flexible chutes are shared (5 between 
Aviator and SGH, 2 between Menzies and SGH, and 2 by all the baggage handlers). There is 
one flight un-allocated in the final solution: flight 184, handled by Aviator with containers and a 
demand of 3 chutes from TP 137 to 175. Compared to S1_11, the solution was not worsen, 
even if the number of flexible chutes was reduced, since there is still only one un-allocation in 
this day, see Appendix E.2. However, compared to S1_15, since there is one flight unallocated 
in S1_9, the objective value was increased by 165%, see Table 42. Additionally, as in Busy, 
SGH is using all of the flexible chutes, meaning that giving an extra chute (or more) to Aviator 
or Menzies is most likely to create at least one un-allocation in that day. 
To conclude on S1_9, the following Table 43 and Table 44 display the repartition of the chutes 
between the three baggage handlers and the flexible chutes during the four days.

It is basically the same results as in S1_11, see Appendix E.2. Therefore, going from 11 flexible 
chutes to 9 flexible chutes did not worsen the optimal solutions. Going further, compared to 
S1_15, there is only one extra un-allocation in Very Busy. In other words, it can be considered 
that reducing the amount of flexible chutes by one third (from 15 to 9) had a minor impact on 
the optimal solutions, and that the layout S1_9 is actually close to an optimal one for BF3. 
Finally, in both Busy and Very Busy all the flexible chutes are shared, see Table 44. And, in 
Medium, Menzies is using all the flexible chutes, whereas, in both Busy and Very Busy, SGH 
is using all the flexible chutes. Based on these observations, it can already be predicted that 
any reduction in the amount of flexible chutes will create new un-allocations. Therefore, it tends 
to show that 9 flexible chutes are actually the optimal number of flexible chutes for BF3 in order 
to solve the CAP. Moreover, as mentioned above, one of the aim of Step 3 is to determine an 
optimal layout for BF3 with 8 flexible chutes. However, based on the results of S1_9, it is 
unclear which baggage handler should be given one extra blue chute. When considering the 
flexible chutes usage, it seems that SGH is the baggage handler that needs one extra chute 
the most. In order to verify this theory, three different layouts were tried where, in each one, 
each baggage handler is given one extra blue chute. The results indeed showed that the best 
solutions were obtained when SGH was given an extra blue chute (best optimal values, 
smallest amount of un-allocations, and smallest amount of chutes shared by all three baggage 
handlers). Therefore, in the following, only the results obtained when SGH is given one extra 
blue chute are displayed, and this new layout is denoted S1_8.

Note that, compared to S1_9, the average amount of two-dimensional objects generated is 
reduced by a factor 1.01, see Table 45. Moreover, compared to S1_9, the amount of un-
allocations was doubled (from 2 to 4 flights unallocated) just by reducing the amount of flexible 
chutes from 9 to 8, see Table 46. In other words, it confirms that 9 flexible chutes are the 
optimal number of flexible chutes for BF3. Additionally, regarding the computation times, 
excluding Low, in average, the solver run for 35 minutes, which is a diminution of a factor 1.2 
compared to S1_9. In the following, the optimal solutions of S1_8 are briefly analysed, and the 
complete optimal results for each day can be seen in Appendix E.2. 
In Low, 86 chutes out of the 109 available are used. More specifically, Aviator is given 18 
chutes, Menzies 8 chutes and SGH 60 chutes. All the flights are allocated, there are no shared 
chutes, and 1 flexible chute is used and given to Aviator. It is basically the same solution as in 
S1_9, which is also confirmed by the fact that the objective value found with S1_8 in Low is 
similar to the one found with S1_9, see Table 46. 
In Medium, 103 chutes are used and, compared to S1_9, there is now one flight un-allocated 
in the optimal solution: flight 248, handled by Menzies with containers and a demand of 3 
chutes from TP 171 to 209. Because of this new un-allocation, the objective value was 
increased by 327% compared to S1_9, see Table 46. 
As for Busy, all the 109 chutes of BF3 are used and, compared to S1_9, the solution was not 
worsen since there is still only one un-allocation: flight 44, handled by SGH with belly wagons 
and a demand of 3 chutes from TP 52 to 90. This is also confirmed by the fact that the objective 
value found with S1_8 in Busy is similar to the one found with S1_9, see Table 46. 
Finally, in Very Busy, all the 109 chutes are used and, two flights are unallocated: 
- 
184, handled by Aviator with containers, demand of 3 chutes from TP 137 to 175. 
- 
195: handled by Aviator with containers, demand of 2 chutes from TP 152 to 180. 
Since there are two flights unallocated in S1_8, the objective value was increased by 58% 
compared to S1_9, see Table 46.

Looking at the above tables, it can be said that Aviator is not using all of its chutes in Medium 
(4 unused chutes) but is given one of the flexible chutes. Therefore, it can be assumed that 
the solver has given one of the flexible chutes to Aviator in order to reduce the total number of 
chutes used in this day. In other words, the usage of one flexible chute by Aviator should be 
avoidable, which would leave two unused flexible chutes in order to allocate flight 248 (in the 
case where the demand of flight 248 would be reduced to 2 chutes). 
To conclude on the results of S1_8, it can be said that, in terms of un-allocations, the results 
are actually identical to what was achieved in the 9th change, see section 7.2.2. To be more 
specific, when looking into the objective values found in both layouts, they are actually almost 
identical, see Table 47. Note that the objective values obtained in S1_8 are actually slightly 
smaller than the ones obtained in the 9th change. This is actually logical since the method 
displayed in this section aims at creating an optimal layout for BF3 starting from scratch. 
Furthermore, when looking at the flexible chutes usage, see Table 48, it can be seen that, if 
one more chute was to be given to one of the baggage handlers, it would have to be to 
Menzies. Indeed, in that case, the solution of both Medium and Very Busy would remain 
unchanged, and only Busy would actually be impacted. Otherwise, giving one extra chute to 
SGH would worsen both Medium and Very Busy, while giving one extra chute to Aviator would 
worsen Medium, and most likely both Busy and Very Busy. 
As mentioned above, the final objective of Step 3 is to create a layout for BF3 with less than 8 
flexible chutes in order to see to what extent it influences the optimal solutions. Based on the 
results of S1_8, a new layout with 7 flexible chutes, S1_7, is created where Menzies is given 
one extra blue chute.

To conclude on the results of S1_7, looking at the flexible chutes usage, see Table 52, it can 
be seen that giving an extra chute to any of the baggage handlers would increase the amount 
of un-allocations in at least two of the four days. More specifically, giving one extra chute to 
Menzies would worsen the solutions in both Busy and Very Busy, giving an extra chute to 
Aviator would worsen the solutions in both Medium and Busy, and giving and extra chute to 
SGH would worsen the solutions in both Medium and Very Busy. In other words, it can be said 
that there is no point in looking for layouts with less than 7 flexible chutes for BF3. 
==PAGE:99==
89 
 
To conclude on Step 3, following Strategy 1 that was devised in Step 2, three layouts were 
created for BF3. More specifically, S1_9 provides a layout with 9 flexible chutes, which is 
considered to be the optimal number of flexible chutes for BF3. S1_8 provides a layout with 8 
flexible chutes, comparable to the original layout of BF3, and actually giving similar results to 
the ones obtained in the 9th change. Finally, S1_7 provides a layout with 7 flexible chutes, and 
it was proven that having less than 7 flexible chutes in BF3 is not a viable option. 
Furthermore, as mentioned at the beginning of Step 3, similar layouts were achieved with 
Strategy 2. Since, in each case, the objective values of Strategy 1 were slightly smaller than 
the ones obtained with Strategy 2, only the results of Strategy 1 were displayed in Step 3. For 
more information on Strategy 2, the different layouts created and the associated optimal 
solutions can be seen in Appendix E.2. 
Finally, as mentioned in Chapter 2, in real-life, no un-allocations can remain in the optimal 
solutions, and RM was introduced in section 6.2.3. This model is entirely based on MM2, and 
allows flights with a demand of 3 chutes to use 2 chutes instead. In the following Step 4, RM 
is applied to the original layout of BF3, and to the four best layouts created in this project (9th 
change, S1_9, S1_8, and S1_7). 
 
Step 4: Reduction Model.

created in this project (9th change, S1_9, S1_8, and S1_7) found when solving the CAP with 
MM2, and compares them to the remaining un-allocations in these layouts after the use of RM. 
For more information, the complete optimal solutions found with RM can be seen in Appendix 
E.2.

As it can be seen in the above Table 53, with the original layout of BF3, RM is only able to 
reduce by one the un-allocations in both Medium and Very Busy. As for the other layouts, RM 
is only removing the un-allocation in Medium, when there is one, and is inefficient in both Busy 
and Very Busy. In other words, it is clear that RM is insufficient and cannot allocate all the 
remaining flights. 
==PAGE:100==
90 
 
The inefficiency of RM can be explained by the fact that, at the times of the un-allocations, all 
the chutes are used and / or that there are not enough flights with a demand of 3 chutes during 
the four days. Indeed, with this technique, in order to allocate the remaining flight(s), there is 
the need for flight(s) with a demand of 3 chutes at roughly the same time as the unallocated 
flight(s). However, in average in Set 1-bis, only 5% of the flights in BF3 have a demand of 3 
chutes. Therefore, compared to MM1 and MM2, the amount of objects generated when using 
RM is only increased by a factor of 1.04, which is not enough in order to make a difference. 
In order to allocate the remaining flights, two different techniques can be used. First of all, RM 
can be modified in order to allow both flights with a demand of 3 chutes and flights with a 
demand of 2 chutes to reduce their demands to respectively 2 chutes and 1 chute. Secondly, 
another technique is to investigate by hand, by looking at the Gantt charts of each day, which 
flights should have their demands reduced in order to allocate the remaining flights. This 
second technique is actually the one that is currently used by the baggage handlers in BF3. 
Given that, in average in Set 1-bis, 27% of the flights in BF3 have a demand of 2 chutes, and 
that the complexity of MM2 is already critical, see section 6.2.4, it is considered that modifying 
RM would worsen the complexity of the model too much. Therefore, all the remaining 
unallocated flights of the four best layouts created in this project (9th change, S1_9, S1_8 and 
S1_7) were allocated by hand using the associated Gantt charts of each day. By doing so, it 
was verified that all the flights of Set 1-bis could actually be allocated in BF3. More specifically, 
in general, when there was a flight with a demand of 3 chutes unallocated, it was allocated by 
reducing the demand of 3 different flights (2 flights with a demand of 2 chutes and 1 with a 
demand of 3 chutes – the unallocated one), and when there was a flight with a demand of 2 
chutes unallocated, it was allocated by reducing the demand of 2 flights with a demand of 2 
chutes (including the unallocated one). The same method could have been applied to the 
original layout of BF3, however, it was decided not to since the amount of un-allocations is 
much greater, see Table 53. Finally, it has to be said that this technique is time consuming and 
is almost out of the scope of the project, and will therefore not be detailed more. Indeed, the 
above unallocated flights were allocated by hand in order to be able to prove that all the flights 
in Set 1-bis can actually be allocated in BF3. To conclude on Step 4, all the remaining flights 
were allocated in the four best layouts created in this project (9th change, S1_9, S1_8 and 
S1_7), which confirms that these layouts are suitable for BF3. In the following, these four 
layouts are referred to as the final layouts for BF3. 
 
 
==PAGE:101==
91 
 
To conclude on section 7.2.3, using MM2 and four different steps, three layouts were created 
for BF3 (S1_9, S1_8 and S1_7). The importance of each of these three layouts can be 
assessed. First of all, it was determined thanks to S1_9 that 9 flexible chutes seem to be the 
optimal number of flexible chutes for BF3. Furthermore, S1_8 provides a layout with 8 flexible 
chutes, comparable to the original layout of BF3 and to the 9th change, see section 7.2.2. The 
characteristics of these three layouts with 8 flexible chutes each are discussed in Chapter 8. 
Finally, S1_7 provides a layout with 7 flexible chutes, and proved that having less than 7 
flexible chutes in BF3 is not a viable option. In order to summarize the results, the following 
Table 54 compares the amount of unallocated flights, the repartition of the chutes between the 
baggage handlers and the computation times for these three final layouts.

Looking at the above Table 54, it logically appears that the more the number of flexible chutes 
is reduced in BF3, the more the amount of unallocated flights increases during the four days, 
and the more the computation times are decreased. Finally, regarding the limits of the method 
displayed in this section, due to the complexity of both the CAP and MM2, it was not possible 
to directly determine optimal layouts for each day of Set 1-bis in Step 1. Therefore, logical 
choices were made in order to devise the three above layouts, and it is not possible to be 
certain that these layouts are actually optimal ones for BF3. 
 
To conclude on section 7.2, and on MM2, several comments can be made. 
First of all, in section 7.2.1, using Set 1-bis on the original layout of BF3, it was proven that the 
repartition of the chutes between the baggage handlers within the different areas is not optimal, 
as foreseen in section 7.1.1. More specifically, green chutes from Aviator were always unused, 
which was creating un-allocations. 
Furthermore, based on the results of section 7.2.1, four new changes were implemented in 
section 7.2.2. It was showed that by simply exchanging chutes from SGH from the blue area 
==PAGE:102==
92 
 
with chutes from Aviator from the green area the optimal solutions were improved (fewer 
unallocated flights and more balanced usage of the flexible chutes, see section 7.2.2). 
Moreover, it was proven that, compared to the amount of chutes given to each baggage 
handler in the original layout, Aviator needs 2 extra blue chutes. All these modifications lead 
to the layout of the 9th change, and it was shown that no other exchanges of chutes were 
possible without worsening the optimal solutions. However, there is actually no way to 
determine whether the layout of the 9th change is an optimal one for BF3. 
Based on the limitations of section 7.2.2, a new approach was therefore considered in section 
7.2.3. The objective was to make MM2 determine the optimal split of the chutes between the 
baggage handlers within the different areas. It was seen that the complexity of both the CAP 
and MM2 is actually too important, and a method in four steps was implemented in order to 
determine three final layouts for BF3 (S1_9, S1_8 and S1_7). 
Lastly, regarding the complexity of MM2, as mentioned in section 6.2.4, the computation times 
can be addressed. For all the layouts tried in section 7.2, the two-dimensional objects, created 
with Java, were all generated in less than 20 seconds. As for the CAP, solved using GAMS, 
depending on the amount of flexible chutes considered, the computation times were between 
14 minutes (8 flexible chutes, see the 9th change) and 1 hour and 20 minutes (15 flexible 
chutes, see S1_15). It is therefore verified that these computations times are higher than the 
ones obtained with MM1, see section 7.1. More specifically, in average with MM2, the 
computations times to generate the two-dimensional objects with Java are increased by a 
factor 2, and the ones to solve the CAP with GAMS are increased by a factor 6, compared to 
the ones of MM1. In agreement with CPH Airport, it is considered that, given the size of MM2, 
these computation times are a bit critical but still acceptable. 
All in all, thanks to MM2, more relevant results were found, compared to section 7.1, and four 
final layouts were devised for BF3: 9th change, S1_9, S1_8 and S1_7. Table 55 summarizes 
the main characteristics of the original layout of BF3 and of the four final layouts.

In Chapter 8, the results of this project are discussed, and ideas of future work are introduced. 
==PAGE:103==

8. DISCUSSION 
The research question of this Master Thesis was: “To what extent can the organization at BF3 
be optimized?”. In agreement with CPH Airport, three main objectives were considered: 
1. 
Determine the optimal number of flexible chutes for BF3 in order to solve the CAP. 
2. 
Assess whether there is / are more adapted layout for BF3 with 8 flexible chutes. 
3. 
Determine whether a layout with less than 8 flexible chutes is a viable option for BF3. 
In order to answer to the research question, the findings and limitations of the project are 
discussed in section 8.1, and some suggestions of future work are introduced in section 8.2. 
8.1 
FINDINGS AND LIMITATIONS OF THE PROJECT 
In this section, the findings and limitations of the project are discussed by going through the 
chapters of the report. 
Chapter 2: 
In Chapter 2, the original layout of BF3 was introduced, and some aspects could already be 
questioned. First of all, all the chutes of Menzies are grouped, except for the green chute 107 
that is alone in the middle of green chutes of Aviator, see section 2.1.1. Considering that, in 
total, Menzies only has 10 chutes, the usage of this chute is highly dependent on the proportion 
of Menzies’ flights handled with belly wagons and with a demand of 1 chute. It was then verified 
that 81% of Menzies’ flights have a demand of 1 chute and that 89% of its flights are handled 
with belly wagons, see section 5.2. In other words, having one green chute alone is not an 
issue for Menzies, but it still appears as an illogical positioning. Another questionable aspect 
of the original layout of BF3 is the organisation of the row that comprises chutes 33 to 51. 
Indeed, chutes 33 to 45 are blue chutes that belong to SGH and chutes 46 to 51 belong to 
Aviator, however only chute 46 is a blue chute, and chutes 47-51 are green chutes. Even if 
53% of Aviator’s flights are handled with containers, only 18% of its flights have a demand of 
1 chute. In other words, it can be considered that chute 46 is mainly used to allocate flights 
from Aviator handled with belly wagons, meaning that chute 46 can be considered as a green 
chute. Furthermore, regarding the organization of the chutes in the different areas, some 
comments can also be made. SGH is allocated all the red area (19 chutes) because they paid 
for its installation, however 53% of Aviator’s flights are handled with containers. Additionally, 
SGH is only given 6 chutes from the green area, which is less than a third of the total amount 
of green chutes (22 chutes), whereas 55% of their flights are handled with belly wagons. All in 
all, the organisation of the chutes in BF3 seems to be mainly dependant on the habits of the 
baggage handlers rather than on a practical usage. Therefore, even if a more optimal usage 
of BF3 can be made, it might be difficult to change the habits of the baggage handlers. 
==PAGE:104==
94 
 
Chapter 5: 
In Chapter 5, it was mentioned that the analysis of the chutes demand did not take into the 
account the adjacency principle and the 2nd repartition constraints (according to the type of 
areas), meaning that, when solving the CAP with all the constraints, the results would be worse 
than what was predicted. Indeed, un-allocations were only foreseen in Very Busy for flights 
handled by Aviator, and section 6.1.1 showed that un-allocations actually occur for all three 
baggage handlers and during three of the four days (Medium, Busy and Very Busy). It can also 
be said that when all the chutes were considered flexible, see section 7.1.3, it was seen that 
there was only one un-allocation left in Busy for a flight handled by SGH. In other words, 
Chapter 5 was unable to foresee that, no matter what the layout of BF3 would be, there would 
always be at least one flight unallocated in Busy. 
Additionally, section 5.3 showed that, for each day of Set 1, the peaks in the chutes demand 
mainly do not overlap for all three baggage handlers. Therefore, in theory, when only the 1st 
repartition constraints (according to the type of chutes) are taken into account, the baggage 
handlers do not need to use the flexible chutes at the same time. In other words, it showed 
that the repartition of the airlines between the three baggage handling companies is well-
thought. However, as it was seen in Chapter 7, when the three sets of constraints of the CAP 
are considered, the three baggage handlers actually mainly need to use the flexible chutes at 
the same time during the days. Therefore, even if the repartition of the airlines between the 
baggage handlers seems optimal, the three sets of constraints of the CAP are actually 
responsible for a difficult management of the flexible chutes. 
Furthermore, section 5.3 showed that in Set 1 there is an average demand of 1.53 chutes per 
flight, whereas in Set 2 there is an average demand of 1.36 chutes per flight. In other words, 
the chutes demand in Set 2 is actually 10% lower than the one of Set 1. Given that Set 2 
comprises the actual chutes demand profile of each flight, it tends to show that, on a daily 
basis, the baggage handlers are reducing the total chutes demand of their flights by 
approximately 10%. Therefore, it raises an important issue regarding the need of this project. 
Indeed, since the baggage handlers are managing the usage of the chutes in BF3 
independently from CPH Airport, it is difficult to assess to what extent they are actually reducing 
the chutes demand of the flights on a daily basis. In other words, if reducing the chutes demand 
of the flights is a common technique for the baggage handlers, or if it is actually done in order 
to be able to avoid using the flexible chutes, it might mean that days that appeared challenging, 
such as Busy and Very Busy in Set 1, are actually not challenging at all in the end, or that 
having 8 flexible chutes in BF3 is sufficient, which would make the results of Chapter 7 less 
relevant. 
==PAGE:105==
95 
 
Finally, with Set 1, CPH Airport provided data on four different days in order to see the 
difference of traffic between unchallenging days (Low) and critical ones (Very Busy). However, 
no data were given regarding the proportion of days in each year that are considered Low, 
Medium, Busy or Very Busy. More specifically, if one type of traffic is constantly witnessed in 
BF3 (e.g. more than two third of the days that would be of Medium traffic), another approach 
to the CAP should be to gather data on days with this specific traffic type only. In other words, 
without knowing the yearly average number of days of each traffic type, there is no way to 
determine whether considering the four days of Set 1 at once when solving the CAP, as it was 
done in Chapter 7, was a good strategy. 
Chapter 6: 
As seen in Chapter 6, one of the objectives of MM2 is to control the usage of the flexible chutes. 
When solving the CAP with MM2, because of the fact that MM2 is a multi-objective model, it 
was actually seen that it is not possible to control and prioritize all the possible usages of the 
flexible chutes. To give an example, a scenario with two flexible chutes and two different 
baggage handlers sharing each of the flexible chutes is considered equal, by MM2, to a 
scenario with two flexible chutes and one baggage handler in one of the flexible chutes and 
the three baggage handlers sharing the other flexible chutes. In order to facilitate the 
management of the flexible chutes, the first scenario should be preferred to the second one. 
Given that there were already four different objectives, and therefore four different penalties, 
in MM2, see section 6.2.2, it was decided not to include these types of consideration. 
Additionally, the complexity of the two mathematical models can be addressed. For all the 
layouts that were tried, depending on the days considered, the amount of two-dimensional 
objects generated with Java was always between 9,000 and 19,000, when the layout tried had 
between 7 and 15 flexible chutes, and between 16,000 and 30,000 when all the chutes were 
considered flexible. In every case, the two-dimensional objects were always generated in less 
than 20 seconds. Therefore, given the three sets of constraints of the CAP and the amount of 
flights in each day, these numbers are reasonable. As for the solving times, it was seen that 
they were only critical for MM2 because of its complexity, see section 6.2.4. Indeed, with MM1, 
for all the layouts with 8 flexible chutes, the CAP was always solved in less than 5 minutes, 
and when all the chutes were considered flexible, the CAP was always solved in less than 20 
minutes. As for MM2, for all the layouts with 8 flexible chutes, in average, excluding Low which 
is not a challenging day, the CAP was solved in 30 minutes, which is actually 6 times longer 
than with MM1. As for layouts where all the chutes were considered flexible, MM2 was unable 
to find optimal solutions within 28 hours. Therefore, it can be considered that the complexity of 
MM2 is the greatest issue of this project, and this aspect is discussed further in section 8.2. 
==PAGE:106==
96 
 
Chapter 7: 
In Chapter 7, four more adapted layouts for BF3 were created: layouts of the 9th change, S1_9, 
S1_8 and S1_7. Some recommendations can be made regarding each of the four layouts. 
First of all, it was shown in section 7.2.3 that 9 flexible chutes are the optimal number of flexible 
chutes for BF3. Therefore, if changes are made in BF3, the layout S1_9 should be the preferred 
one. However, compared to the original layout, using the layout S1_9 would mean creating an 
extra flexible chute. In the case where CPH Airport would be willing to remain with 8 flexible 
chutes only, the layout S1_8 should be chosen. Additionally, using the layouts S1_9 or S1_8 
would also require to move all the last minute chutes, which would probably be costly for both 
CPH Airport and the baggage handlers. In the case where CPH Airport would be willing to 
keep the last minute chutes in the same position as in the original layout, the layout of the 9th 
change should be chosen. Indeed, in this layout, the last minute chutes are in the same 
positions as in the original layout, and there are 8 flexible chutes. Additionally, regarding the 
position of the flexible chutes, the 9th change is actually closer to the original layout, compared 
to the layout S1_8. To be more specific, the flexible chutes are placed in chutes 56-57, 67-68, 
and 97-100 in the original layout, in chutes 56-57, 68-69 and 89-92 in the 9th change, and in 
chutes 39-43 and 93-95 for the layout S1_8. In other words, using the layout S1_8 would mean 
changing the position of all the flexible chutes, and even using another row, whereas using the 
layout of the 9th change would only require to change the position of 5 of the flexible chutes. 
Finally, regarding the layout S1_7, it was only created in order to see what would happen in 
BF3 with less than 8 flexible chutes, and it was proven in section 7.2.3 that using less than 7 
flexible chutes is not a viable option for CPH Airport. To conclude on these four layouts, in 
case of changes in BF3, the layout S1_9 should be the preferred one, followed by the layout 
S1_8, then by the layout of the 9th change, and finally by the layout S1_7. 
Moreover, the repartition of the chutes in the two layouts with 8 flexible chutes created in 
Chapter 7 (9th change and S1_8) can be compared to the one of the original layout of BF3 
since it also has 8 flexible chutes. First of all, the results obtained in the 9th change and in S1_8 
are actually similar. Indeed, there is the same amount of un-allocations in each day (0 in Low, 
1 in Medium, 1 in Busy and 2 in Very Busy), and the objective values are similar. More 
specifically, the objective values of S1_8 are slightly smaller than the ones of the 9th change, 
which can be explained by the fact that the layout S1_8 was achieved by letting MM2 decides 
on the optimal split of the chutes, see section 7.2.3, whereas the layout of the 9th change was 
achieved thanks to modifications by hand based on the original layout of BF3, see section 
7.2.2. It is therefore logical that the objectives values of S1_8 are slightly smaller than the ones 
of the 9th change. The following Table 56 compares the repartition of the chutes between the 
baggage handlers in the different areas of BF3 for these three layouts with 8 flexible chutes. 
==PAGE:107==

Looking at Table 56, it can be seen that the total amount of chutes given to each baggage 
handler is identical in both the 9th change and S1_8, and that, compared to the original layout, 
Aviator is given 2 more chutes and SGH 2 less. As for the repartition of the chutes within each 
area, several comments can be made. First of all, for all three layouts, the red area entirely 
belongs to SGH. A layout where the red area is shared between Aviator and SGH was also 
tried with Strategy 2 (layout S2_8, see Appendix E.2), but the results showed that Strategy 1, 
and therefore the layout S1_8, is more adapted to BF3 than Strategy 2, see section 7.2.3. As 
for the green area, the key change lies in the increase of green chutes given to SGH in both 
the 9th change and S1_8, compared to the original layout of BF3. Indeed, in the original layout, 
SGH is given 27% of the green chutes, and respectively 68% and 73% in the 9th change and 
in S1_8. Finally, regarding the blue area, it can be seen that both Aviator and Menzies are 
given more chutes there in both the 9th change and S1_8 compared to the original layout, 
which is logical given the changes that occurred in the green area. More specifically, Aviator 
is given 13% of the blue area in the original layout, 30% in the 9th change and 27% in S1_8, 
and Menzies is given 7% of the blue area in the original layout, 8% in the 9th change and 13% 
in S1_8. All in all, it can be said that, compared to the original layout of BF3, it is the repartition 
of the chutes between the baggage handlers within each area that was highly modified in both 
the 9th change and S1_8, and not the total amount of chutes given to each baggage handler. 
Furthermore, in Step 1, see section 7.2.3, when all the chutes were considered flexible and 
MM2 was used in order to find the optimal split of the chutes between the baggage handlers 
in BF3, the solver run 28 hours and was unable to find optimal solutions. It can be discussed 
whether it would have been worthy to leave the solver run for more time in order to find the 
optimal solutions. To do so, the gap between the objective values that were found in Step 1 
and the ones of the associated LP relaxation can be addressed, see Table 31 in section 7.2.3. 
In Low there is no gap since the optimal solution was found, in Medium there is a gap of 91%, 
in Busy there is a gap of 13%, and in Very Busy there is a gap of 99%. It can be said that in 
Medium and Very Busy the gap is too important, meaning that letting the solver run more than 
28 hours would most likely be ineffective. Additionally, 28 hours is already a critical 
computation time. This time was actually chosen in order to have exploitable results in Step 1. 
==PAGE:108==
98 
 
Additionally, as mentioned in Chapter 7, when solving the CAP using Set 2, it was realised that 
this dataset was not challenging enough for BF3. To be more specific, for the seven days of 
Set 2, and using the original layout of BF3, there were no un-allocations, and all the 109 chutes 
of BF3 were never used, see Appendix F.1. Therefore, the data of Set 2 were slightly modified 
in order to make them more challenging for BF3, and Set 2-bis was created, see Appendix F.2. 
In Set 2-bis, the chutes demands of the flights were increased in order to reach an average 
demand of 1.57 chutes per flight (1.53 in Set 1, and 1.36 in Set 2, see above), and the 
repartition of the chutes demand of the flights was also kept similar to the one of Set 1, see 
Appendix F.2 for more details. The CAP was then solved with Set 2-bis for the original layout 
of BF3, and for three of the four layouts created in Chapter 7 (9th change, S1_8 and S1_7), 
and all the results are displayed in Appendix F.2. To conclude on Set 2-bis, with the original 
layout of BF3, un-allocations were created (7 un-allocations in total), and these un-allocations 
were actually reduced when the layouts created in Chapter 7 were used (1 un-allocation in 
total with the layouts of both the 9th change and S1_8, and 3 un-allocations in total with the 
layout S1_7), see Appendix F.2. Therefore, it was confirmed with Set 2-bis that the layouts 
created in Chapter 7 are more adapted to BF3 than the original one. 
Finally, even if four more appropriate layouts for BF3 were created in Chapter 7 (9th change, 
S1_9, S1_8, and S1_7), actually implementing them would be a challenge for CPH Airport. 
More specifically, moving last minute chutes (as it is required with the layouts S1_9, S1_8 and 
S1_8) and reorganizing the repartition of the chutes in the green and blue areas (as it is 
required in all four layouts) would compel the baggage handlers to move their equipment, and 
change their working habits. CPH Airport would have to determine the amount of time, and 
money, that these types of layout reorganisations require, and therefore assess whether it 
would be worthy compared to the changes in the habits of the baggage handlers it would imply. 
 
To conclude on this section, it can be said that the research question of this project has been 
answered. More specifically, it was determined that the optimal number of flexible chutes 
necessary in BF3 in order to solve the CAP is 9 flexible chutes, and the layout S1_9 was 
created. Additionally, two layouts with 8 flexible chutes, more adapted than the original layout 
of BF3, were created: the layout of the 9th change and the layout S1_8. Finally, it was 
determined that a layout with 7 flexible chutes is an option for BF3, and the layout S1_7 was 
created. However, it was seen that a layout with 8 flexible chutes should be preferred to one 
with 7 flexible chutes, and that having less than 7 flexible chutes in BF3 would not be a viable 
option for CPH Airport. In the following section 8.2, some ideas of future work are introduced. 
 
==PAGE:109==
99 
 
8.2 
FUTURE WORK 
First of all, in section 2.1.3, it was mentioned that there are only three ways for the baggage 
handlers to allocate their flights. More specifically, a flight can be allocated to chutes that 
belong to its own baggage handler and are in a suitable area (i.e. respect the loading type of 
the flight, containers or belly wagons), or to chutes that are flexible, or to a combination of 
chutes that belong to its own baggage handler (and are in a suitable area) and of chutes that 
are flexible. Future research could investigate to what extent the optimal solutions of the CAP 
would be improved if flights could also be allocated to chutes that belong to their own baggage 
handler but with an incorrect loading flight (e.g. a flight handled with container that would be 
allocated to chutes in the green area). To include this change in MM2, a new penalty term 
would have to be added to the objective function, and more two-dimensional objects would be 
created for each flight. Therefore, it should also be assessed to what extent this modification 
of MM2 would influence the complexity of the CAP. 
Moreover, as mentioned in section 8.1, the critical point of this project is the complexity of 
MM2. Future research should investigate ways to decrease its complexity by rethinking entirely 
MM2 and / or by looking into other heuristics. Indeed, in this project, the CAP was solved with 
GAMS using CPLEX, and only the branch and cut procedure was considered (CPLEX, n.d.). 
Furthermore, Chapter 7 showed that, except for Low that is not a challenging day, each day of 
Set 1 is critical for one of the baggage handlers only (Medium for Menzies, Busy for SGH and 
Very Busy for Aviator). Therefore, these days were all considered at once when creating new 
layouts for BF3. Another idea that could be investigated would be to choose one of the days 
of Set 1 as a base, and determine a more appropriate layout for BF3 only based on this day, 
and see to what extent it influences the optimal solutions of the remaining days of Set 1. 
Additionally, as mentioned in Step 4 of section 7.2.3, with RM, flights with a demand of 3 chutes 
are allowed to reduce their demand to 2 chutes. The results when using RM were inconclusive, 
and the remaining unallocated flights were allocated by hand using Gantt charts, see section 
7.2.3. Another idea of future work would be to modify RM in order to allow both flights with a 
demand of 3 chutes, and flights with a demand of 2 chutes, to reduce their demands to 
respectively 2 chutes and 1 chute. The key point would be to ensure that the reduction of the 
chutes demand of flights with a demand of 2 chutes would be penalized more than the one of 
flights with a demand of 3 chutes. It should also be assessed to what extent this modification 
of RM would increase the complexity, and therefore influence the computation times. 
Moreover, as mentioned in section 2.1.2, when several of the baggage handlers have to use 
the same flexible chute during the day, some set-up times have to be considered. Indeed, the 
==PAGE:110==
100 
 
baggage handling companies are using different software and equipment, which is why one of 
the objectives of MM2 was to minimize the sharing of the flexible chutes. It was explained by 
CPH Airport that, in order to facilitate the management of the flexible chutes, and therefore 
avoid too many different set-up times during the day, the baggage handlers are usually given 
all of the flexible chutes at once and for a definite period of time of the day (e.g. the morning). 
However, when looking at the Gantt charts of the flexible chutes of the optimal solutions found 
with MM2 for the original layout, see Appendix C.2, it can be seen that this kind of usage of 
the flexible chutes cannot be respected. Indeed, in Very Busy for instance, around TP 75, 
chutes 67 and 68 that were used by Aviator are then allocated to SGH with no set-up time 
possible, see Table 64 in Appendix C.2. Additionally, at that same time period, the other flexible 
chutes are actually shared between Aviator and SGH (5 are used by Aviator and 1 by SGH). 
Therefore, an idea of future work would be to modify MM2 in order to be able both to include 
set-up times for the flexible chutes, and to provide schedules of the flexible chutes that respect 
the above characteristics (give all the flexible chutes at once for a definite period of the day to 
the baggage handlers). 
As it was mentioned in section 8.1, it seems that this project was too theoretical regarding the 
chutes demand of the flights, and that the baggage handlers are actually reducing the total 
chutes demand of their flights by approximately 10% in each day in order to limit their usage 
of the flexible chutes. An idea for future work could be to interview the baggage handling 
companies in order to see both to what extent they actually reduce the chutes demand of their 
flights, and how they generally use the flexible chutes on a daily basis. However, since the 
baggage handlers are using the chutes in BF3 independently from CPH Airport, this kind of 
research would rely entirely on a voluntary collaboration from the baggage handlers. 
Finally, future research could consist in a cost analysis project in order to assess whether it 
would be worthy for CPH Airport to make the baggage handlers use the same equipment so 
that all the chutes of BF3 could be shared. Indeed, if all the baggage handlers were using the 
same equipment, the first set of constraints of the CAP (according to the type of chutes) would 
be dropped, and there would be no need to minimize the sharing of the chutes in BF3, as it 
was done with MM2. In this case, the objective of the CAP could be to minimize the distance 
between the chutes chosen to allocate the flights and their associated airplane. This objective 
would actually be more interesting for both the baggage handlers and CPH Airport since it 
would facilitate the work of the baggage handlers by making them gain time. However, it would 
be extremely costly for the baggage handlers to change all their equipment, and being able to 
share all the chutes of BF3 would also require a great change regarding their habits. 
==PAGE:111==
101 
 
9. CONCLUSION 
 
This project focused on the Chute Allocation Problem (CAP) at CPH Airport in the baggage 
factory BF3. The research question of this Master Thesis was: “To what extent can the 
organization at BF3 be optimized?”. 
To answer to the research question, two mathematical models were implemented, MM1 and 
MM2. To be more specific, MM1 is able to solve the CAP in BF3 and ensure that a maximum 
number of flights are allocated in the optimal solutions. However, MM1 is unable both to 
provide any information regarding the chutes usage, and to minimize the sharing of the flexible 
chutes. For these reasons, MM2 was created, and it is able to control both the usage of the 
chutes by the three baggage handling companies and the sharing of the flexible chutes, while 
still ensuring that a maximum number of flights are allocated in the optimal solutions. Indeed, 
thanks to new sets of variables, MM2 is able to display, for each chute of BF3, which baggage 
handler(s) is / are using it. Therefore, when a chute is flexible, MM2 also displays how many 
baggage handlers are sharing the chute during the day. 
Furthermore, two different approaches were followed during this project. The first approach 
consisted in implementing modifications by hand to the original layout of BF3, and see to what 
extent the optimal solutions of the CAP were improved. With the first approach, the layout of 
the 9th change was created. Based on the limitations of this approach, a second approach was 
devised where all the chutes of BF3 were considered flexible. The aim of this approach was to 
let MM2 decide on the optimal split of the chutes between the three baggage handlers in each 
area of BF3, and on the minimum number of flexible chutes necessary to solve the CAP. With 
the second approach, three layouts were created, layouts S1_9, S1_8 and S1_7. All in all, 
thanks to the two above approaches, four more adapted layouts for BF3 were created, layouts 
of the 9th change, S1_9, S1_8 and S1_7. 
To conclude, with the layout S1_9, it was determined that the optimal number of flexible chutes 
necessary in BF3 in order to solve the CAP is 9 flexible chutes. Moreover, thanks to the two 
above approaches, two layouts with 8 flexible chutes, more adapted than the original layout of 
BF3, were created, layout of the 9th change and layout S1_8. Finally, with the layout S1_7, it 
was determined that a layout with 7 flexible chutes is also an option for BF3. All in all, it can be 
said that CPH Airport was satisfied with the results of the project, and it is considered that the 
research question of this project has been answered.

APPENDIX 
 
A. REWARD PROFILES OF MATHEMATICAL MODEL 1 
As mentioned in Chapter 2, there are only three possible ways to allocate a flight: 
1. It can be allocated to chutes that belong to its own baggage handler and are in a suitable 
area (red or blue if it is a flight handled with containers, and green or blue if it is a flight 
handled with belly wagons). 
2. It can be allocated to the flexible chutes. 
3. It can be allocated both to chutes that belong to its own baggage handler are in a suitable 
area, and to chutes that are flexible (only if these two types of chutes are adjacent). 
 
Furthermore, six reward profiles can be created: 
1. 
Flight handled by SGH with containers 
2. 
Flight handled by SGH with belly wagons 
3. 
Flight handled by Aviator with containers 
4. 
Flight handled by Aviator with belly wagons 
5. 
Flight handled by Menzies with containers 
6. 
Flight handled by Menzies with belly wagons 
 
Additionally, as mentioned in section 6.2.2, when a flight is allocated to chutes in BF3, a reward 
is given for each chute that is used. Since at CPH Airport flights can have a demand of up to 
3 chutes, up to 3 rewards can be given per flight. Depending on where the flights is actually 
allocated, four types of reward can be distinguished: 
1. ∝1: Reward given to a baggage handler when it uses one of its own chutes to allocate a 
flight. 
2. ∝2: Reward given to a baggage handler when it uses the closest flexible chute to its own 
chutes to allocate a flight. 
3. ∝3: Reward given to a baggage handler when it uses the second closest flexible chute to 
its own chutes to allocate a flight. 
4. ∝4: Reward given to a baggage handler when it uses another of the flexible chutes to 
allocate a flight. 
These rewards are actually the only possible coefficients of the reward matrix 𝑅𝑓 𝑐 introduced 
in section 6.2.2. 
 
==PAGE:116==
2 
 
In agreement with CPH Airport, it was chosen to have: ∝1 = 2, ∝2 = 1.5, ∝3 = 1, and ∝4= 0.5. 
The six possible reward profiles are displayed in the following Figure 20 to Figure 23 using the 
current layout of BF3. To facilitate the reading, the rewards are denoted A1 (for ∝1), A2 (for 
∝2), A3 (for ∝3), and A4 (for ∝4). 
 
As an example, flight 39 of Set 1 introduced in section 5.1 is handled by SGH with containers 
and has a demand of 2 chutes from TP 60 to 86. Using the above Figure 20, there are three 
possible ways to allocate this flights: 
1. Use 2 chutes that belong SGH from the red or the blue area: 
For instance, using the chutes 89 and 90 in the final solution (see section 2.1.1 for the 
chutes numbering) would give the reward 𝑅𝑜= 𝑅39,89 + 𝑅39,90 = 𝛼1 + 𝛼1, see Figure 20. 
2. Use 2 of the flexible chutes: 
For instance, using the chutes 97 and 98 in the final solution (see section 2.1.1 for the 
chutes numbering) would give the reward 𝑅𝑜= 𝑅39,97 + 𝑅39,98 = 𝛼2 + 𝛼3, see Figure 20. 
3. Use 1 of the flexible chute and 1 of the chutes that belong to SGH in the blue area: 
For instance, using the chutes 96 and 97 in the final solution (see section 2.1.1 for the 
chutes numbering) would give the reward 𝑅𝑜= 𝑅39,96 + 𝑅39,97 = 𝛼1 + 𝛼2, see Figure 20. 
Figure 20: First reward profile, flights handled by SGH with containers (A1: ∝1, A2: ∝2, A3: ∝3, A4: ∝4). 
==PAGE:117==
3 
 
Therefore, considering the three above scenarios, the use of chutes 89 and 90 that belong to 
SGH would give the greatest reward, and the use of the flexible chutes 97 and 98 would give 
the smallest reward.

this.type=type;

}

}

public class Flight { 
 
private int id; 
 
private int demand; 
 
private String handler; 
 
private int open; 
 
private int duration; 
 
private String type; 
 
private int reward; 
 
 
 
public void setID(int id){ 
 
 
this.id=id; 
 
} 
 
public void setDemand(int demand){ 
 
 
this.demand=demand; 
 
} 
 
public void setHandler(String handler){ 
 
 
this.handler=handler; 
 
}

this.open=open; 
 
} 
 
public void setDuration(int duration){ 
 
 
this.duration=duration; 
 
} 
 
public void setType(String type){ 
 
 
this.type=type; 
 
} 
 
public void setReward(int reward){ 
 
 
this.reward=reward; 
 
} 
 
 
public int getID(){ 
 
 
return id; 
 
} 
 
public int getDemand(){ 
 
 
return demand;

GAMS code to solve the CAP: 
* Allocation.gms 
$eolcom // 
option 
iterlim=999999999; 
 
 
 
 
// 
avoid 
limit 
on 
iterations 
option reslim=7000;            // timelimit for solver in sec. 
option 
optcr=0.0; 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
// 
gap 
tolerance 
option solprint=ON;           // include solution print in .lst file 
option limrow=100;            // limit number of rows in .lst file 
option limcol=100;            // limit number of columns in .lst file 
//-------------------------------------------------------------------- 
Sets // the size of o and f depends on the day (here: Busy); 
o /0*30067/ 
f /1*350/ 
c /1*109/ 
t /0*287/ 
 
Parameters Aof(o,f); 
$ include Aof.txt 
Parameters Boct(o,c,t); 
$ include Boct.txt 
Parameters Rfc(f,c); 
$ include Rfc.txt 
// Reward of using object o 
Parameters R(o); 
R(o)= sum(f, sum(c, sum(t, Rfc(f,c)*Aof(o,f)*Boct(o,c,t) ))); 
// Penalty of un-allocating one flight 
Parameter lambda; 
lambda=2; 
 
Variables 
x(o) Is 1 if object o is part of the final solution 
y(f) Is 1 if flight f is unallocated in the final solution 
z 
; 
Binary variables 
x 
y 
; 
Equations 
obj 
eq1(f) 
eq2(c,t) 
; 
obj .. z =e= sum(o, R(o)*x(o)) - sum(f, lambda*y(f)); 
// When the chutes are all considered flexible: 
//obj .. z=e=  - sum(f, lambda*y(f)); 
eq1(f).. sum(o, Aof(o,f)*x(o)) + y(f) =e= 1; 
eq2(c,t) .. sum(o, Boct(o,c,t)*x(o)) =l= 1; 
 
Model Allocation /all/; 
Solve Allocation using mip maximizing z; 
execute_unload "results.gdx" x.L y.L; // Export the results to an Excel file 
execute 'gdxxrw.exe results.gdx var=x.L rng=A1' 
execute 'gdxxrw.exe results.gdx var=y.L rng=A4' 
display x.L; 
display y.L;

import java.util.*; 
import java.io.*; 
 
public class Gantt{ 
public static void main(String[] args) throws IOException { 
 
long start = System.currentTimeMillis(); 
 
// Extracting data from the object_info file generated in the 1st Java code 
BufferedReader reader=new BufferedReader(newFileReader("object_info.txt")); 
String line = null; 
Scanner scanner = null; 
int index1 = 0; 
List<object_low> objectList = new ArrayList<>(); 
 
while ((line = reader.readLine()) != null) { 
 
object_low obj = new object_low(); 
 
scanner = new Scanner(line); 
 
scanner.useDelimiter(";"); 
 
while (scanner.hasNext()) { 
 
 
String data = scanner.next(); 
 
 
if (index1 == 0) 
 
 
 
obj.setobjectID(Integer.parseInt(data)); 
 
 
else if (index1 == 1) 
 
 
 
obj.setflightID(Integer.parseInt(data)); 
 
 
else if (index1 == 2) 
 
 
 
obj.setchuteID(Integer.parseInt(data)); 
 
 
else if (index1 == 3) 
 
 
 
obj.setStart(Integer.parseInt(data)); 
 
 
else if (index1 == 4) 
 
 
 
obj.setLength(Integer.parseInt(data)); 
 
 
else if (index1 == 5) 
 
 
 
obj.setHandler(data); 
 
 
else 
 
 
 
System.out.println("invalid data::" + data); 
 
 
index1++; 
 
} 
index1 = 0; 
objectList.add(obj); 
} 
reader.close(); 
 
// Extracting data from the optimal solution found with GAMS (objects ID)

while ((line_l = reader_l.readLine()) != null) {

scanner_l.useDelimiter(";"); 
 
while (scanner_l.hasNext()) { 
 
 
String data_l = scanner_l.next(); 
 
 
final_obj.setfinalobjectID(Integer.parseInt(data_l)); 
 
} 
final_objList.add(final_obj); 
} 
reader_l.close();

Parameters Boct(o,c,t);

Parameters pcd(c,d); 
$ include pcd.txt 
Parameters 
*Penalty of unallocating one flight: 
lambda1 
*Penalty of having more than one handler per chute: 
lambda2 
*To counterbalance the reward given by pcd: 
lambda3 
; 
lambda1=500; 
lambda2=10; 
lambda3=3; 
 
Variables 
x(o) Is 1 if object o is part of the final solution 
y(f) Is 1 if flight f is unallocated in the final solution 
v(i,c) Is 1 if handler i is using chute c in the final solution 
w(c) Nb of extra handlers using chute c (0 if c is unused or 1 handler only) 
p(c) For big M equations with w(c) 
q(i,c,d) Is 1 when both v(ic) and v(id) are 1 
z 
;

20 
 
Binary variables 
x 
y 
v 
p 
q 
; 
Integer variables 
w 
; 
 
Equations 
obj 
eq1(f) 
eq2(c,t) 
M1(c,i) 
M2(c,i) 
M3(c) 
M4(c) 
M5(c) 
M6(c) 
M7(i,c,d) 
M8(i,c,d) 
; 
 
obj..z=e=sum(c,sum(d$(ord(c)<ord(d)),sum(i,q(i,c,d)*pcd(c,d)))) 
-sum(f,lambda1*y(f))-lambda2*sum(c, w(c))-lambda3*sum(i, sum(c, v(i,c))); 
 
eq1(f).. sum(o,Aof(o,f)*x(o))+ y(f) =e= 1; 
eq2(c,t)..sum(o,Boct(o,c,t)*x(o)) =l= 1; 
M1(c,i)..1-sum(o,sum(f,Aof(o,f)*Coc(o,c)*dif(i,f)*x(o))) =l= 100*(1-v(i,c)); 
M2(c,i)..sum(o, sum(f, Aof(o,f)*Coc(o,c)*dif(i,f)*x(o))) =l= 100*v(i,c); 
M3(c)..2 - sum(i, v(i,c)) =l= 100*p(c); 
M4(c)..-1 + sum(i, v(i,c)) - w(c) =l= 100*p(c); 
M5(c)..w(c) =l= 100*(1-p(c)); 
M6(c)..sum(i, v(i,c)) - 1 =l= 100*(1-p(c)); 
M7(i,c,d)..q(i,c,d) =l= 100*v(i,c); 
M8(i,c,d)..q(i,c,d) =l= 100*v(i,d); 
 
Model Allocation /all/; 
Solve Allocation using mip maximizing z;

import java.util.*; 
import java.io.*; 
 
public class Allocation { 
public static void main(String[] args) throws IOException { 
 
long start = System.currentTimeMillis(); 
 
// Extracting data from the chute file 
BufferedReader reader_c = new BufferedReader(new FileReader("Chute.csv")); 
String line_c = null; 
Scanner scanner_c = null; 
int index1 = 0; 
List<Chute> chuteList = new ArrayList<>(); 
 
while ((line_c = reader_c.readLine()) != null) { 
 
Chute chute = new Chute(); 
 
scanner_c = new Scanner(line_c); 
 
scanner_c.useDelimiter(";"); 
 
while (scanner_c.hasNext()) { 
 
 
String data_c = scanner_c.next(); 
 
 
if (index1 == 0) 
 
 
 
chute.setID(Integer.parseInt(data_c)); 
 
 
else if (index1 == 1) 
 
 
 
chute.setHandler(data_c); 
 
 
else if (index1 == 2) 
 
 
 
chute.setType(data_c); 
 
 
else if (index1 == 3) 
 
 
 
chute.setEnd(Integer.parseInt(data_c)); 
 
 
else 
 
 
 
System.out.println("invalid data::" + data_c); 
 
 
index1++; 
 
} 
 
index1 = 0; 
 
chuteList.add(chute); 
} 
reader_c.close();

//Generating the multi-dimension objects 
int index_o = 0; 
 
for(Flight f:flightList){ 
 for (int t=0; t<288; t++){ 
  if (f.getOpen()== t){ 
   for (Chute c:chuteList){ 
    if (c.getHandler().equals(f.getHandler())|| 
        c.getHandler().equals("NONE")){ 
     if (c.getType().equals(f.getType()) || c.getType().equals("NONE")){ 
 
if (c.getEnd()-c.getID()+1 >= f.getDemand()){ 
 
  int c_demandadded = c.getID() + f.getDemand() -1 ; 
 
  Chute cbis = new Chute(); 
 
  cbis = chuteList.get(c_demandadded-1); 
 
  if (cbis.getHandler().equals(f.getHandler()) || 
            cbis.getHandler().equals("NONE")){ 
 
    if (cbis.getType().equals(f.getType())|| 
              cbis.getType().equals("NONE")){ 
 
 
  Aof.println("Aof('"+ index_o+ "','" +f.getID()+"') = 1;"); 
 
       for (int tbis=t; tbis<t+f.getDuration();tbis++){

for(Flight f:flightList){

dif.println("dif('2','" + f.getID() +"') = 1;");

 dif.println("dif('3','" + f.getID() +"') = 1;");

if (c.getID()<c.getEnd()){ 
Chute c_next = new Chute(); 
c_next = chuteList.get(c.getID()); 
 
 
if (c.getHandler().equals("NONE")) 
 
 
 pcd.println("pcd('"+c.getID()+"','"+c_next.getID()+"') = 1;");
 
 
else if (c_next.getHandler().equals("NONE")) 
 
 
 pcd.println("pcd('"+c.getID()+"','"+c_next.getID()+"') = 1;"); 
 
 
else if (c.getHandler().equals(c_next.getHandler())) 
 
 
 pcd.println("pcd('"+c.getID()+"','"+c_next.getID()+"') = 2;"); 
} 
} 
 
Aof.close();

Coc.close();

System.out.println("The execution time is "+((end-start)/1000d)+"seconds");

$ include pcd.txt 
Parameters Po(o); 
$ include Po.txt 
Parameters 
*Penalty of unallocating one flight 
lambda1 
*Penalty of having more than one handler per chute 
lambda2 
*To counterbalance the reward given by pcd 
lambda3 
*To counterbalance the use of 2 chute objects instead of 3 chute objects 
lambda4 
; 
lambda1=500; 
lambda2=10; 
lambda3=3; 
lambda4=50; 
 
Variables 
x(o) Is 1 if object o is part of the final solution 
y(f) Is 1 if flight f is unallocated in the final solution 
v(i,c) Is 1 if handler i is using chute c in the final solution 
w(c) Nb of extra handlers using chute c (0 if unused or 1 handler only) 
p(c) For big M equations with w(c) 
==PAGE:140==
26 
 
q(i,c,d) Is 1 when both v(ic) and v(id) are 1 
r(o) Is 1 if reduction of chutes from 3 to 2 
z 
; 
 
Binary variables 
x 
y 
v 
p 
q 
r 
; 
 
Integer variables 
w 
; 
 
Equations 
obj 
eq1(f) 
eq2(c,t) 
M1(c,i) 
M2(c,i) 
M3(c) 
M4(c) 
M5(c) 
M6(c) 
M7(i,c,d) 
M8(i,c,d) 
Reduc(o) 
; 
 
obj .. z =e= sum(c,sum(d$(ord(c)<ord(d)),sum(i,q(i,c,d)*pcd(c,d)))) 
+lambda4*sum(o,Po(o)*x(o))-sum(f,lambda1*y(f))-lambda2*sum(c,w(c)) 
-lambda3*sum(i,sum(c,v(i,c))); 
 
eq1(f).. sum(o, Aof(o,f)*x(o))+ y(f) =e= 1; 
eq2(c,t) .. sum(o, Boct(o,c,t)*x(o)) =l= 1; 
M1(c,i) .. 1-sum(o,sum(f,Aof(o,f)*Coc(o,c)*dif(i,f)*x(o)))=l=100*(1-v(i,c)); 
M2(c,i) .. sum(o, sum(f, Aof(o,f)*Coc(o,c)*dif(i,f)*x(o))) =l= 100*v(i,c); 
M3(c) .. 2 - sum(i, v(i,c)) =l= 100*p(c); 
M4(c) .. - 1 + sum(i, v(i,c)) - w(c) =l= 100*p(c); 
M5(c) .. w(c) =l= 100*(1-p(c)); 
M6(c) .. sum(i, v(i,c)) - 1 =l= 100*(1-p(c)); 
M7(i,c,d) .. q(i,c,d) =l= 100*v(i,c); 
M8(i,c,d) .. q(i,c,d) =l= 100*v(i,d); 
Reduc(o) .. r(o) =e= - Po(o)*x(o); 
 
Model Allocation /all/; 
 
Solve Allocation using mip maximizing z;

execute 'gdxxrw.exe results.gdx var=x.L rng=A1'

From the above results, it can be seen that among all the week there are no flights unallocated 
in the optimal solutions. Additionally, the 109 chutes of BF3 are never all used (at most 100 
chutes used), whereas the flexible chutes are almost always all used. Indeed, during all the 7 
days both Menzies and Aviator are using the flexible chutes (mainly to accommodate container 
flights) whereas they are in almost all the week not using all the chutes at their availability in 
the green area. 
All in all, it can be said that these results emphasize the need to rethink the organization of the 
chutes between the baggage handlers in the different areas, as it was seen in Chapter 7 with 
Set 1. Furthermore, as there are no un-allocations with this set, the following section aims at 
creating a more challenging set for BF3, Set 2-bis, based on the data of Set 2, and see to what 
extent it influences the optimal solutions in BF3.

As it can be seen in the above tables, even if there are more flights in average in Set 2, the 
average chutes demand per flight is greater in Set 1: 1.53 chutes per flights in Set 1 and 1.36 
chutes per flight in Set 2. Additionally, it can be seen that the main difference between the two 
datasets lies within the repartition of flights with a demand of 1 and 2 chutes. Indeed, in 
percentages, there are more flights with a demand of 1 chute and less flights with a demand 
of 2 chutes in Set 2, compared to Set 1. Therefore, in order to create a more challenging set 
for BF3 based on Set 2, the idea is to increase the chutes demand of several flights (mainly 
increase flights with a demand of 1 chute to 2 chutes) in order to reach a similar repartition of 
==PAGE:199==
85 
 
the chutes demand as in Set 1. This process was done on Set 2 and Set 2-bis was created. In 
average among the week, here are the increase of chutes demand that were realised: 
- 
Aviator: 9 flights with a demand of 1 chute increased to 2 chutes. 
- 
Menzies: 4 flights with a demand of 1 chute increased to 2 chutes, and 2 flights with a 
demand of 1 chutes increased to 3 chutes. 
- 
SGH: 47 flights with a demand of 1 chute increased to 2 chutes. 
The following table presents the repartition of the chutes demand of the flights for each 
baggage handler for Set 2-bis.

Compared to the original layout of BF3, the usage of the chutes is also more appropriate with 
the layout S1_8. Indeed, in general, the baggage handlers (mainly Aviator) are using their own 
chutes before using the flexible chutes, meaning that the repartition of the chutes within each 
area is more adequate. Additionally, as with the layout of the 9th change, all of the un-
allocations, except one from SGH in the 14.04.2014, were resolved with this layout. Finally, it 
can also be said that compared to the layout of the 9th change, the layout S1_8 seems more 
performant. Indeed, in total there are less shared chutes (20 shared chutes in the 9th change 
and 17 shared chutes with S1_8), and there are less chutes unused from the baggage handlers 
(in total 12 unused chutes from all the baggage handlers in the 9th change and 7 unused chutes 
with S1_8).

It can be seen that with the layout S1_7 there are 3 un-allocations in total during the week (2 
more than in the 9th change and in S1_8) which confirms that 8 flexible chutes is more 
appropriate for BF3 than 7 flexible chutes. Additionally, it can be mentioned that even with 7 
flexible chutes only, there are less un-allocations in S_7 than with the original layout of BF3 (7 
un-allocations in total). 
 
All in all, it can be said that the results found with Set 2-bis corroborate the ones found in 
Chapter 7 with Set 1 and Set 1-bis. However, it has to be kept in mind that Set 2-bis is artificial 
and was created based on both Set 1 and Set 2.
